| Executive |     | Summary |     |     |     |     |
| --------- | --- | ------- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
Labor market positions are simultaneously determined and deterministic. During and
after episodes of economic shocks, it is not uncommon to see spikes in both macro-
and individual-level measures of employment uncertainty including unemployment,
fixed-term employment, and informality, among others. At the same time, these
experiences of employment uncertainty have profound implications for demographic
behavior and other life course outcomes. During the peak of the COVID-19 pandemic,
for instance, birth rates fell in most countries, and whether these short-run fluctuations
translate to long-run and cohort-specific effects remains to be seen. Recognizing these
interrelationships, thisdissertationisacollectionofessaysexploringnarrowerquestions
| around the | concept    | of employment | uncertainty.  |         |            |          |
| ---------- | ---------- | ------------- | ------------- | ------- | ---------- | -------- |
| What       | do we mean | by            | uncertainty”? |         | summarizes | insights |
|            |            | “employment   |               | Chapter | 1          |          |
from seminal and contemporary empirical literature, revisits existing typologies and
operationalizations of this concept, and demonstrates the overarching framework
of this dissertation. This chapter also presents updated estimates of macro-level
associations between measures of employment uncertainty (e.g., unemployment,
vulnerable employment, and self-employment rates) and total fertility rates using
| dynamic | panel models. |     |     |     |     |     |
| ------- | ------------- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
How did stringent COVID-19 restrictions affect informal employment?
Chapter
2 leverages the regional variation in the imposition of extreme COVID-19 lockdowns
in the Philippines to estimate its effects on informal employment. Using a difference-
in-differences design on pooled Labor Force Surveys, the paper shows that extreme
lockdowns increase the probability of informal employment for women more than men.
This effect is most pronounced for mothers with minor children, highlighting how some
women during the pandemic played a dual role as workers and carers of first resort.
In the analysis, a conceptual and empirical distinction is made between compositional
| and survivalist | informalization. |     |     |     |     |     |
| --------------- | ---------------- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
i

ii
How do initial (uncertain) employment conditions affect subsequent fertility
behavior? Chapter 3 leverages inter- and intra-cohort differences in employment
conditions to estimate its effects on fertility behavior within the first ten years after
labor market entry. Using the German Socio-Economic Panel, the paper identifies
initial employment uncertainty in two ways— working on a fixed-term contract and
starting during a recession. To partly address endogenous selection into initial
conditions, the paper employs a two-step identification strategy combining an optimal
full matching step and an event history modeling step using the matched data. The
paper demonstrates that women who enter with a fixed-term contract and males who
enter during a recession experience lower first birth hazards up to a decade after entry.
Are there class-based differentials in the transition to second-order births?
Chapter 4 analyzes the association between occupational class membership and
second-birth transitions in Germany, testing whether workers from higher social classes
possess an “economic advantage” for fertility decisions. Using event history models on
data from the German Socio-Economic Panel, the paper reveals this to be the case.
Men and women in service classes, as well as those who experience upward career
mobility post-first birth, experience strongly elevated second-birth rates.
How do future caregiving responsibilities and employment uncertainties affect
fertility decisions? Departing from the past- and present-orientedness of the preceding
chapters,Chapter 5analyzestheeffectsoffuture caregivingandemploymentscenarios
and narratives on current ascribed fertility intentions. Using a factorial survey
experiment on a sub-sample from the German Socio-Economic Panel, respondents
are randomly exposed to hypothetical scenarios of couples with varying levels of
future caregiving responsibilities and employment uncertainties. Results show that
couples without these responsibilities and uncertainties have higher fertility intentions
relative to when these are high. Further testing for heterogeneities by the respondents’
gender and own employment markers, results lend support to a gendered difference
in the effect of future caregiving responsibilities, whereas neither objective nor
subjective measures of respondents’ employment markers moderate the effect of future
employment uncertainty.

school is a solid support system within and beyond the university. Though I wasn’t the
smartest student back then, I was entrusted with this opportunity and felt supported
along the way. I would compare my PhD journey to sailing a ship, where each part
played a crucial role. Along these functional lines, I thank everyone who’s profoundly
impacted me and my experience as a PhD student.

| Chapter | 1   |     |     |     |     |     |
| ------- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
Introduction
|     |     |     | “The concept | of         | class is organized |       |
| --- | --- | --- | ------------ | ---------- | ------------------ | ----- |
|     |     |     | around       | one common | principle—it       | is    |
|     |     |     | the kind     | of chances | in the market      | that  |
|     |     |     | determines   | the common | conditions         | of    |
|     |     |     | individuals’ | fate.”     |                    |       |
|     |     |     |              |            | -Max               | Weber |
|     |     |     | Waters       | et al.     | (2010) translation |       |
What affects people’s labor market positions and how do these positions shape
subsequent demographic behavior? A broad and relevant question that spanned
centuries of thinkers, its re-examination is warranted in an era of post-crisis
recovery, pronounced demographic changes, and scientific advances in analyzing causal
relationships. Yet such a question warrants a more nuanced contextualization–there is
perhaps no uniformity neither to our understanding of what constitutes employment
uncertainty nor its centrality in shaping life course outcomes. The recent COVID-
19 pandemic revealed that people’s labor market positions (e.g., whether they are
unemployed, in short-time work, informally employed, etc.) are, on the one hand,
partly determined by the severity of the pandemic and its accompanying policies and,
on the other hand, significant determinants of demographic outcomes such as fertility
and parenting intensity (Sobotka et al. 2023; Naujoks et al. 2022; Maurizio et al. 2023;
| Chodorow-Reich | and Coglianese | 2021). |     |     |     |     |
| -------------- | -------------- | ------ | --- | --- | --- | --- |
However, the directionality and magnitude of these relationships likely vary based
on context and subpopulation groups. In prior work analyzing how recessions affect

2 CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
fertility, women in cohorts that entered the labor market during the 1998 Asian
FinancialCrisisinSouthKoreaexperiencedlowercompletedfertility(Choietal.2020),
while women labor market entrants during the 1980 recession in the US eventually had
higher completed fertility rates relative to comparable non-recession cohorts (Schwandt
and von Wachter 2020). This difference can be attributed to various non-mutually
exclusive reasons. One could point to a cultural or contextual explanation that
women in the US and South Korea during those times might differently perceive the
importance of economic factors in childbearing decisions. Another reason could be
that the magnitude of the two recessions isn’t exactly comparable— the negative effect
of the 1997-98 Asian Financial Crisis on the South Korean economy far outweighed
the negative effect of the 1980 recession on the US economy, at least when looking
at GDP growth rates. Finally, it could also simply be an empirical reflection of the
competing income and substitution effects in the economic analysis of fertility (Becker
1960). Recognizing the thick strand of research in this regard, this dissertation builds
on the idea that more often than not, employment conditions matter even for non-labor
market outcomes such as childbearing decisions.
Core to this dissertation is the argument that employment uncertainty is both
a determined and a deterministic phenomenon. It is determined in the sense
that most measures of employment uncertainty exhibit countercyclical patterns—
unemployment, informality, fixed-term employment, short-time work, and subjective
job worries, among others, tend to increase during economic downturns. This is not
a new idea—standard aggregate demand-aggregate supply models in economics posit
that as a response to an exogenous shock in demand (for example, due to the COVID-
19 lockdowns), aggregate output is depressed and this ripples through lower demand
in input markets including for both capital and labor. When firms demand less labor,
how and why does employment uncertainty rise? One simple and obvious answer is
the rise in unemployment due to layoffs. Unemployed people, by definition, are willing
and available to work but cannot look for one and, consequently, are in an uncertain
labor market position. Among developing countries, however, another risk-coping
strategy during recessionary periods is to engage in informal work instead, another
type of uncertain employment. Given the difficulties of finding opportunities in the
formal sector, some people are constrained to work informally in temporary, irregular,
low-paid, and sometimes precarious conditions. This “survivalist motive” also partly
explains how uncertain employment conditions are themselves outcomes determined

3
by exogenous shocks and crises.
Employment uncertainty is likewise deterministic— future family formation and
labor market outcomes are in part a function of past, present, and future employment
uncertainty. For instance, the Global Financial Crisis, which unambiguously increased
rates of employment precarity at least in the short-run, affected not only individual
labor market trajectories but also family formation outcomes, especially in Europe and
the US. Looking at population-level measures, Comolli et al. (2021) show that there
has been a steeper decline in marriage rates and a halt on increasing fertility rates in
Nordic countries during and after the 2008-09 crisis. In a comparative context, Comolli
and Bernardi (2015) find a permanent positive effect of the recession on childlessness
among women in their late 30s in the US but Caltabiano et al. (2017) does not find
this effect to hold for Italy. Apart from this, the relationship between business cycles
and fertility has also been extensively studied in the literature from both a macro
and a micro perspective (see Caltabiano et al. 2017; Hofmann et al. 2017; Comolli
and Bernardi 2015; Kreyenfeld and Andersson 2014, for example). Recently, more
attention has been devoted to the explicit framing of economic uncertainty as an idea
or perception of a “future” narrative (Vignoli et al. 2020a; Lappegård et al. 2022).
Understanding employment uncertainty as both determined and deterministic,
this dissertation is a collection of four stand-alone chapters broadly on its causes
and demographic consequences. The rest of this chapter motivates the importance
of this topic and the selection of specific contexts as well as presents broader trends
and patterns of employment and fertility. In Chapter 2, informal employment is
demonstratedtoincreaseasaresultofextremelockdownenforcementinthePhilippines
butonlyforwomen, particularlymotherswithminorchildren. Thisevidencehighlights
women’sdualroleasworkersandcarersoffirstresort. InChapter3, initialemployment
conditions are demonstrated to affect subsequent fertility behavior– female fixed-term
entrants and male recession entrants have lower first birth hazard rates within the
first 10 years of entry, relative to their permanent and non-recession counterparts.
These findings imply the persistent nature of initial conditions that affect later life
outcomes. In Chapter 4, occupational class positions are shown to be a relevant
gradient of higher-order births such that men and women in relatively economically
advantaged service classes experience elevated second birth rates relative to workers in
lower occupational classes. Finally, in Chapter 5, future employment uncertainty and
forthcoming caregiving responsibilities are demonstrated to negatively affect fertility

4 CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
intentions, implying that multiple and simultaneous experiences of uncertainty hamper
and threaten the enabling conditions of initiating parenthood.
1.1 Operationalizing Employment Uncertainty
Subtly highlighted in the preceding section is that employment uncertainty is best
understood as an umbrella term that comes in various shapes and forms. By
textual definition, “uncertainty” simply involves imperfect, incomplete, or unknown
information. An informally employed worker, for instance, has no certainty that s/he
will have a job the next day nor that s/he will receive a salary by a certain date
absent any job contract. Similarly, a fixed-term worker has no certainty that s/he will
receiveacontractextensionorapermanentpositionuponthecompletionofthecurrent
contract. The commonality between these two workers is that their current labor
market positions inherently involve uncertainties that, say, their formally employed
and permanently employed counterparts do not. In some spaces in the labor literature,
uncertainty is interchangeably used with “precarity”, both denoting a certain degree of
inferiority and involuntariness relative to non-uncertain positions. While the social
stratification literature has thoughtfully threshed out and defined varieties and levels
of precariousness within the labor market (see Standing 2012, for example) dating back
to Weber et al. (1922) and other earlier thinkers, there still seems to be no collective
or universal definition of what constitutes employment uncertainty.
Among the more recent attempts to schematize uncertainty is that of Blossfeld
etal.(2006)whocharacterizethreetypesof(overall)uncertainty—economic,temporal,
and employment. The first refers to the uncertainty of one’s employment and
educational circumstances, the second refers to the growing preference against long-
term binding commitments, and the last refers to one’s job stability owing to being in
the public sector (versus private) and with a permanent contract (versus fixed-term).
While this schema appropriately constructs the conditions of what makes a certain
socio-economic position uncertain in a hyper-globalized era, it can be argued that it is
mainly limited by its dimensions. The types of uncertainty in this schema are far too
few to capture its other manifestations. For instance, in most developing countries,
informal working arrangements are more of the norm rather than the exception and
these carry way more uncertainty than formally employed workers with automatic
enrollment and access to social security. Further, uncertainty may also emanate from

1.1. OPERATIONALIZING EMPLOYMENT UNCERTAINTY 5
occupational class positions as skilled classes benefit from more opportunities and
better working conditions as economies transform and increasingly become service-
oriented.
Thus, thissectionattemptstoexpandourcollectiveunderstandinganddescription
of uncertainty, capturing not only positional markers of labor market status (e.g.,
employed vs. unemployed vs. inactive) but also objective markers of employment
arrangements and subjective indicators of uncertainty. Table 1.1 summarizes the
various forms and typologies specifically of employment uncertainty and each is
subsequently discussed in more detail.
Table 1.1: Forms and Typologies of Operationalizing Employment Uncertainty
Type of Uncertainty Forms and Categories
- Employed

Positional Uncertainty. In official labor market statistics, the working-age
population is typically classified into one of three states– employed, unemployed, and
inactive. These individual states are mutually exclusive labor market positions, of
whichsomeinherentlypossessmoreuncertaintyrelativetoothers. Frominternationally
harmonized definitions, unemployment refers to people willing and available to work,
and looking for work but cannot find one. In comparison with people who want
to and are available to work and are currently working (i.e., employed) and people
who neither prefer nor are able to work and are not looking for work (i.e., inactive),
unemployed people are relatively in more uncertain positions. Positional uncertainty
in this dissertation thus encompasses the inherent uncertainty of the job search process
experienced by being in a state of unemployment. In prior literature examining the

6 CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
unemployment-fertility nexus, some have used macro-level measures of unemployment
rates at the national or subnational level (Sobotka et al. 2011; Currie et al. 2014;
Matysiak et al. 2021a; Schneider 2015), whereas some used individual-level states of
unemployment (Kreyenfeld 2010; Andersen and Özcan 2021; Adsera 2011).
Durational Uncertainty. While many “typical” employment contracts have an
open-ended and unlimited duration (with some countries offering strong protections
from layoffs), a sizable segment of the employed population works in time-bound
or term-limited arrangements without any guarantee of renewal, extension, or
regularization upon its expiration. Paradoxically, having a fixed-term contract carries
less uncertainty on one hand in that there is clarity and precision as to one’s
employment duration but, on the other hand, more uncertainty in terms of future job
prospects and, in some contexts where contractual workers have less or no coverage,
access to certain entitlements (e.g., parental leaves, health insurance, etc.). Kalleberg
(2000) argues that fixed-term workers in the US contend with elevated turnover rates
and heightened job instability, thereby perpetuating a sense of insecurity, financial
strain, and anxiety among fixed-term workers. In Germany, fixed-term workers have
been shown to have lower earnings, lower subsequent wage growth, and higher risks
of future unemployment relative to permanent contract workers (Giesecke 2009). The
same wage penalties for fixed-term workers within a decade post-entry have likewise
been demonstrated in Australia (Fauser and Mooi-Reci 2024). In this dissertation,
durational uncertainty thus refers to the inherent perceived and actual instability
and precarity of being in a fixed-term contract A durational operationalization of
employment uncertainty using fixed-term contracts is common and has been done
in prior literature investigating its effects on subsequent fertility behavior (Auer and
Danzer 2016; Schmitt 2012, 2021; Gebel and Giesecke 2009; Vignoli et al. 2020b).
Statutory Uncertainty. A majority of workers in low and lower-middle-income
economies are in informal employment arrangements that are either not covered or
governed by employer-employee contracts or not declared to tax and public insurance
authorities or both. Take for example the case of casual street vendors or freelance
output-based workers—these are classified as employed in official statistics but do not
receive the same types of protection and entitlements as formally employed workers
do. There are various definitions of informal employment across countries and to date,
there is no universally recognized operationalization of who is considered informally
employed. Benanav (2019) offers a historical account of this lack of harmonization and

1.1. OPERATIONALIZING EMPLOYMENT UNCERTAINTY 7
points to the ILO’s evolving constructions and definitions of “informal employment”
and “employment in the informal sector”. The absence of contractual arrangements and
properstatutorydeclarationsandregistrations(e.g., taxfilings)ofinformallyemployed
workers makes them inherently susceptible to less protection and access to entitlements
(e.g., insurance coverage), if at all existent. In terms of the personal repercussions
of informal employment, Karabchuk (2020) shows that these workers express lower
fertility desires while Ducanes et al. (2023) find that they have lower wages and longer
work hours, and are more likely to have no rest days and work below minimum wage
rates.
Recessionary Uncertainty. Employment uncertainty likewise has a cyclical
element in that the timing of labor market entry per se matters. All other employment
conditions held constant, recession cohorts have been demonstrated in the literature
to have lower wage trajectories (von Wachter 2020b,a; Oreopoulos et al. 2012),
and different family formation patterns (Hofmann and Hohmeyer 2016; Raymo and
Shibata 2017; Choi et al. 2020). The uncertainty experienced by recession entrants is
multi-faceted. First, there are fewer job opportunities during periods of economic
downturns, thereby potentially lengthening job search and lowering initial wages.
Second, depending on the magnitude and severity of these downturns, the negative
effects on subsequent wage and employment outcomes may persist up to decades
after entry, thereby generating cohort-specific “scarring” phenomena (Rothstein 2021;
Huckfeldt 2022). Beyond labor market entrants, the fertility decisions of individuals
are negatively affected by other indicators of macroeconomic uncertainty including
Google searches of the term “spread” (Comolli and Vignoli 2021) and media coverage
of economic news (Guetto et al. 2023b).
Class-Based Uncertainty. Emanating from the Weberian distinction of
types of stratification between a prestige-based “Stände” (Status) and material or
resource-based “Klasse” (Class) (Weber et al. 1922), decades of succeeding literature
has operationalized class schema using occupations and demonstrated inter-class
differentials in both attitudinal and behavioral outcomes including employment,
political beliefs, cultural consumption, and family formation (Erikson et al. 1979a;
Chan and Goldthorpe 2007; Goldthorpe and McKnight 2006; Baizan 2021; Kreyenfeld
etal.2023b). Centraltocontemporaryoperationalizationofsocialclassisitsanchoring
on occupational categories, assuming that people within the same class are relatively
similar in terms of their material resources and exposure to economic and political

| 8   |     |     |     |     | CHAPTER | 1. INTRODUCTION |

cycles. In Erikson et al. (1979a, 1983), class positions are classified into nine-, seven-
, five-, and three-fold schemas, where distinctions are made between the “service”
class that encompasses workers in managerial and professional occupations and the
“unskilled” class representing workers in manual and routine occupations including
farm work. Thus so, this dissertation also adopts a class-based operationalization of
employmentuncertaintywhereinworkersinlower-classpositionsareexposedtogreater
levels of economic insecurity and susceptibility to economic trends and fluctuations
(e.g., offshoring, recessions, automation, etc.) that service-class workers may not

Subjective Uncertainty. Perhaps the most straightforward measure of
uncertainty is one’s subjective worries about their labor market positions. Rather than
makingclass-basedorcategory-basedimputationsofuncertaintythatpresupposeintra-
class homogeneity, subjective measures of job worries are direct and able to capture
individual-level differences. Many publicly available representative surveys contain

of employment uncertainty pertain to differences within paid employment categories,
there are of course uncertainties emanating from unpaid work as well. Caregiving
responsibilities, either toward children or elderly parents, are strongly gendered and
cause observable penalties in employment outcomes and subjective well-being. Kleven
et al. (2023) demonstrate that across the world, motherhood penalties in terms of
employment rates are pronounced and persistent, although these are smaller for
low-income countries. Understandably so, intergenerational coresidence being more
common in these contexts and the abundance of local domestic workers and caregivers
may cushion the employment impacts of motherhood. In terms of overall caregiving
work, Schäper et al. (2023) shows that women perform around twice more than men
do. The presence of caregiving responsibilities, therefore, generates uncertainty in that

1.2. EMPLOYMENT UNCERTAINTY AS A GLOBAL PHENOMENON 9
caregivers face greater time demands outside of paid employment than non-caregivers.
This consequently affects their employability, available work hours, health outcomes,
and even fertility decisions (e.g., having a child/another child). It is important to note
that while caregiving responsibilities arguably increase burden of workers and their
personal precarity, it is also inherently uncertain. As in the case of caring for a sick
parent, for instance, there is genuine uncertainty about when this need would arise,
how much caregiving is required, and how long this caregiving responsibilities persist.
Notethatthisschemaisanextensionoftheschematicillustrationofuncertaintyin
prior work. While Blossfeld et al. (2006) characterize uncertainty as either economic,
temporal, or employment, this section narrowly focuses on employment uncertainty,
expands its typologies and markers, and incorporates different terms that may be more
appropriate descriptions. For instance, what Blossfeld et al. (2006) coin as “economic”
is called “positional”, whereas “employment” is termed “durational”.
1.2 Employment Uncertainty as a Global
Phenomenon
Employment uncertainty is a pervasive global phenomenon explained by non-mutually
exclusive factors including globalization, technological change, business cycles, and
concentration, among others. Indeed, the COVID-19 pandemic highlighted the
fragility of global labor markets, causing widespread displacement estimated to be
approximately 255 million full-time jobs (Berg et al. 2021). Regardless of how
employment uncertainty is operationalized, it can be argued that it is generally a
countercyclical phenomenon that increases during periods of economic shocks. This
section shows historical trends and patterns of employment uncertainty and a closer
look at the Philippine labor market, one of the world’s most significantly impacted
countries during the COVID-19 pandemic.
1.2.1 Trends in Employment Uncertainty
Despite schematically outlining the typologies of uncertainty in the preceding
section, the greater challenge lies in measurement. Whereas some measures have
internationally agreed-upon definitions and harmonized estimates (e.g., unemployment
rate, identifying recession years, etc.), all others either do not have common definitions

10 CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
(e.g., informal employment) or are not regularly and consistently measured (e.g.,
fixed-term employment, class schemas, subjective worries, and caregiving). Thus, to
illustrate global trends we rely on ILO-harmonized historical estimates of two measures
ofemploymentuncertainty–(1)unemploymentrate(asashareofthetotallaborforce);
and(2)vulnerableemploymentrate(asashareoftheemployedpopulation). Whilethe
former straightforwardly refers to the share of the labor force looking for, available, and
willing to work but can’t find one, vulnerable employment is an ILO-formed concept
referring to the share of the employed population who are contributing family workers
and own-account workers. To some extent, this definition from the ILO captures what
we mean with the term “statutory uncertainty” in the preceding section (informally
employed vs. formally employed workers). This ILO measure captures workers who
are in undeclared, informal, or piece-rate work (e.g., street vendors, domestic workers,
etc.) and has the advantage of being harmonized across many countries.
Figure 1.1: Global Trends in Unemployment Rate (1991-2022)
Note: IncomegroupsarebasedonthelatestclassificationbytheWorldBankusingpercapita
Gross National Income while regional groups are based on the World Bank’s administrative
divisions. HarmonizedILOestimatesofunemploymentratesareusedtoensurecomparability.
In Figure 1.1, it is interesting to note how the cyclicality of unemployment
rates is more pronounced among high-income economies— most notably, the spike in
unemployment for this group during the Global Financial Crisis in 2008-09 which was
notreflectedamonglowandlower-middleincomeeconomies. Thesameobservationcan
be made when comparing regional groups. On the contrary, the COVID-19 pandemic
did cause unemployment spikes across all regions and income groups, albeit in different
magnitudes. Interestingly, low-income countries have, on average, lower unemployment

1.2. EMPLOYMENT UNCERTAINTY AS A GLOBAL PHENOMENON 11
rates than high-income countries. Since the latter typically have more extensive social
welfare systems, including income replacement programs and other forms of social
assistance, they can also lead to higher reported unemployment rates compared to low-
income countries. In these contexts, displaced workers in low-income countries may
either not be counted as part of the labor force if they are not actively seeking work,
consequently shrinking the size of the labor force and the reported unemployment rate
or they may transition towards some kind of informal work since they cannot afford
unemployment, thereby minimizing observable transitions to unemployment.
Figure 1.2: Global Trends in Vulnerable Employment Rate (1991-2022)
Note: IncomegroupsarebasedonthelatestclassificationbytheWorldBankusingpercapita
Gross National Income while regional groups are based on the World Bank’s administrative
divisions. Harmonized ILO estimates of vulnerable employment rates are used to ensure
comparability. This rate covers contributing family workers and own-account workers as a
percentage of total employment.
With regard to the size of the vulnerably employed population, Figure 1.2 shows
stark differences across income and regional groups, as expected. Whereas high-
income and developed economies, in general, have persistently low shares of vulnerable
employment, a vast majority of workers in low and lower-middle-income countries are
in vulnerable arrangements. In South Asia and Sub-Saharan Africa, they comprise
about 80% of the employed workers and have remained at that level for over 3
decades. This category encompasses various forms of informal employment and other
precarious arrangements that leave workers vulnerable to exploitation, poverty, and
social exclusion. By definition, vulnerable workers have limited access, if at all, to
social safety nets, including unemployment insurance, disability benefits, or pension
schemes, making them all the more susceptible to shocks.

12 CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
Looking at the figure more closely, it is interesting to note that by and large, the
vulnerable employment rate (VER) is not that sensitive to business cycle fluctuations.
Evenamonghigh-incomeeconomieswhereunemploymentspikesarepronouncedduring
past periods of crises, VER has been relatively stable. This implies that even if the
employed population shrinks, its composition (between vulnerable and non-vulnerable
workers) remains stable. However, there seem to be some exceptions to this pattern.
Among lower-middle-income economies and in South Asia, East Asia and the Pacific,
Sub-Saharan Africa, and Latin America, there seems to be a very small increase in the
VER in 2020.
1.2.2 Country-in-Focus: Philippines
These observations from Figure 1.2 on the VER dynamics in 2020, at the height
of the COVID-19 pandemic, necessitates the importance of decoupling the effect of
a certain shock (e.g., COVID-19) on unemployment and on the size of vulnerable
(or informal or precarious) employment. Indeed, one can observe a “compositional”
increaseinVEReveniftheemployedpopulationshrinksifthedeclineinnon-vulnerable
is larger than the decline in vulnerable employment. The COVID-19 pandemic and its
ensuing lockdowns present a unique opportunity to better understand the dynamics of
vulnerable and/or informal work in the presence of an exogenous shock.
What happens to informal employment in developing countries during economic
downturns? Looking at historical accounts could be an insightful starting point. There
issomeconsensusthatinformalemploymentincreasesasaresponsetoeconomicshocks
(Albertini et al. 2019; Colombo et al. 2019). During and after financial crises episodes
in Latin American countries in the 1990s, the Argentine peso crisis in 2001, and its
spillovers in Uruguay in 2002, informal employment rates increased and remained high
for years succeeding the crises (Colombo et al. 2019). This implies that during a
recession when demand for labor is depressed, involuntary employment serves as a
cushion for job displacement which workers can do rather than be unemployed or
inactive. Since then, there have been other, more severe, crises that emerged such as
the COVID-19 pandemic.
The second chapter of this dissertation takes a closer look at how informal
employment in the Philippines responded to the imposition of extreme lockdowns
in select regions. The Philippines is widely considered to have imposed one of the
world’s longest and strictest lockdowns that were enforced in part through military

1.2. EMPLOYMENT UNCERTAINTY AS A GLOBAL PHENOMENON 13
action (Reuters 2020; Olanday and Rigby 2020; See 2021), making it an interesting
and relevant case study. The central thesis in this dissertation is that (statutory)
employment uncertainty, operationalized using informal employment, is determined
by both economic uncertainty (e.g., the COVID-19 pandemic or the Asian Financial
Crisis, both of which depressed aggregate demand) and the ensuing state responses
(e.g., the imposition of extreme lockdowns or the adoption of austerity policies).
Figure 1.3: Labor Market Indicators in the Philippines, by Gender (1991-2022)
Note: These indicators are all based on ILO estimates.
As is the case in most other contexts, the Philippine labor market is visibly
gendered and, in select indicators, has been increasingly so in recent years. Figure
1.3 shows 4 indicators— labor force participation, which is the sum of employed
and unemployed people as a share of the working-age population, unemployment,
vulnerable employment, and self-employment rates. Most notably, women have
persistently lower labor force participation rates and higher unemployment rates,
vulnerable employment rates, and over the last decade, also self-employment rates.
Thus, more women are situated in uncertain labor market positions. The gendered

14 CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
effect of the COVID-19 pandemic is barely visible in these descriptive figures— while
the spike in unemployment in 2020 was larger for women than men, the increases in
vulnerable and self-employment rates do not seem substantially larger for either sex.
There are limited possibilities to infer causal claims from these descriptive figures.
For one, these estimates are aggregated at the national level, while government
responses to the pandemic, such as the imposition of lockdowns, have been region-
specific. Thus, this dissertation tests the question of whether employment uncertainty,
measured through informal employment, is indeed determined by workers’ exposure
to extreme and militarized lockdowns and, if so, whether such effect is gendered.
1.3 Demographic Repercussions of Employment
Uncertainty
1.3.1 Seminal Theories on the Role of Economic Conditions
Malthusian proposition. The idea that demographic behavior, and population
dynamics more broadly, is a function of employment conditions is recurrent and
historically well-examined. Malthus (1798) contends that there is a cyclicality (or
“oscillations” as he puts it) between population growth and economic conditions and,
considering that (geometric) population growth outpaces (arithmetic) food production
growth, this cycle would eventually lead to poverty and famine. Touching on the
interrelationships between scarcity, labor productivity, and family formation, central to
theMalthusianpropositionisthatwhenapopulationincreasesandthereismoresupply
of labor than the “proportion of work in the market”, wages fall. As a consequence of
theselow-wagedemploymentconditionsthatnecessitatedlongerworkhours, marriages
and births fall such that the population will stall, exerting upward pressure on wages
and improvements in economic conditions. Family formation then becomes conducive
and the population will increase, ultimately repeating the dismal cycle (Malthus 1798).
Malthus (1798) proposes solutions or “checks” to limit population growth and
prevent famine and hardships— positive and preventive checks. The former are
events that increase mortality (and decrease population growth) including famine,
disease, war, and other natural disasters. These consequently increase death rates
in a population, helping balance the size of the population and the available resources.
These checks were regarded as inevitable to prevent population growth from exceeding

1.3. DEMOGRAPHIC REPERCUSSIONS OF EMPLOYMENT UNCERTAINTY15
the carrying capacity of the environment. However, Malthus also recognized the
accompanying suffering caused by these events. On the other hand, preventive checks
are individual or social measures to consciously control population growth and limit
births including late marriage, celibacy, and contraception. Unlike positive checks
that affect mortality, preventive checks reduce population growth through a decline
in fertility. Malthus (1798) believes that preventive checks are preferable because
they offer a more humane means of controlling population growth. Ultimately,
moral restraint and self-discipline are central to limiting family size. The dismal
predictions of widespread famine and poverty due to unchecked population expansion
have been recurrent, most notably with Ehrlich (1968) illustrating scenarios of future
food insecurity and mass starvation as a result of the rapid population growth in the
20th century. Broadly, the Malthusian observation that family formation is negatively
affected by a decline in wages implies that births are directly affected by labor market
conditions.
Boserupian response. Contrary to the rather pessimistic implications of the
Malthusian proposition, Boserup (1981) emphasizes human ingenuity, technological
innovation, and resilience towards the challenges posed by population growth.
Specifically, she argues that historically, societies innovated and developed new farming
techniques such as crop rotation, irrigation, terracing, and animal cultivation to meet
the increased demand for food. Therefore, increasing population densities serve as
sufficient incentives for technological change rather than a precursor to eventual
hardship. Another difference of the Boserupian argument is the role that social
institutions play in facilitating innovation that causes some societies to adopt better,
whereas Malthus’ observations contain little to no nuance based on the type and
structureofasociety. Inrelationtoemploymentconditions, Boserup(1981)showsthat
growing societies historically responded to increased demand for food particularly by
employing more labor-intensive farming methods and technologies. Therefore, another
implication of her work is that population growth could lead to increased employment
opportunities in the agricultural sector. Contemporary scholars have carried on this
argument— “human ingenuity” is an abundant and fast-growing resource that could
innovate society out of scarcity (Simon 1981). Similarly, Lam (2011) contends that
despite the seeming success of the increase in world food production, some regions,
particularly Africa, have been less successful, owing to both social institutions and
resource constraints.

16 CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
Economic models. The economic analysis of fertility in a rational choice
framework emanates from the seminal work of Becker (1960, 1981) where childbearing
decisions are modeled similarly to consumption goods and the trade-off occurs between
the quantity and quality of children. Thus, changes in income and relative prices
ultimately determine fertility behavior. As a response to an improvement in income
(or employment conditions), the “income effect” captures its positive relationship with
fertilitysuchthathavinghigherincomesandmoreresourcesisanenablingconditionfor
family formation. On the other hand, a “substitution effect” as a response to an income
increase captures how individuals may reduce fertility decisions due to the increased
opportunity cost of childbearing. Empirically, however, Becker (1960) observes that
there is a lack of a strong income effect in the income-fertility nexus and explains
this by introducing a “quality” component in the model. Simply put, an increase in
income might be spent either on another child or additional investments in existing
children. Overall, his work highlights the role of economic incentives and constraints
in influencing fertility behavior and, since then, subsequent literature has extended
this theory (see Wrede 2011, for example, where hyperbolic discounting is introduced
in the model.).
Relatedly,Easterlin(1976),bestknownforthe“EasterlinParadox” ofincomelevels
and happiness, posits in the same seminal paper that whereas there is no relationship
between those two factors, there is a (positive) relationship between income levels and
fertility. He observes that fertility rates tend to be higher during periods of economic
prosperity and lower during downturns, as shown by the post-World War II baby boom
in the United States, which coincided with a period of economic growth and increased
job opportunities. On the contrary, settlements with lower land availability for farming
are shown to be associated with lower fertility.
Ineconomics, thereseemstobeashiftinthetheoreticalunderpinningsofstudying
fertility choices. An extensive review by Doepke et al. (2023), who studied under
Gary Becker (Doepke 2015), highlights the “new economics of fertility” where evolving
family structures and gender roles play a more central role. Doepke et al. (2023)
emphasizes that whereas fertility used to decrease with income and female labor force
participation in the past, now, among high-income economies, the association of these
two variables with fertility is positive. He argues that part of what explains this is the
shift from the quantity-quality tradeoff to a career-family tradeoff— women in high-
income countries at present aspire for both career AND family stability. With this

1.3. DEMOGRAPHIC REPERCUSSIONS OF EMPLOYMENT UNCERTAINTY17
development, the importance of labor markets becomes even more central since high-
unemployment societies might convert temporary caregiving breaks of mothers to long-
termunemploymentand/orinactivity. This“neweconomics” brandthusrecognizesthe
important role of both labor market and family policies (e.g., parental leave, subsidized
childcare, and family planning, among others) in explaining fertility dynamics. In prior
work, Doepke et al. (2019) shows that parenting intensification, where parents increase
time and material investments towards their children, plays a role in the lowering
of fertility rates. The more recent economics literature on employment conditions
and fertility has therefore evolved into one that better takes into consideration social
policies and changing norms.
Demographic Transition Theories. While economic fluctuations explain
fertility decisions, these theories (at least the older ones) are incomplete in that they
do not offer nor incorporate the role of ideational change in demographic developments
such as cohabitation and secularization (Lesthaeghe 2010; Zaidi and Morgan 2017).
The first and second demographic theories (FDT and SDT, respectively) incorporate
botheconomicandlabormarketconditionsononehandandchangesinideasandnorms
on the other, to provide a fuller account of why and how societies transitioned from
high fertility and mortality to low fertility and low mortality ones (FDT) and from low
fertility societies to “lowest-low” fertility ones (SDT). In explaining the demographic
changes in Western Europe from the 18th to the 20th century, Notestein (1945, 1952)
shows that societies changed from high to low fertility and mortality in part because of
significant improvements in healthcare and sanitation as well as industrialization and
urbanization. Another important element during the FDT is the shrinkage of family
sizes, shifting towards smaller nuclear families. These reasons, however, are perhaps
insufficient to explain the shift of low fertility societies to even lower levels of fertility.
The SDT (Lesthaeghe and Van de Kaa 1986; Lesthaeghe 2010) thus emphasizes
three components— structure, culture, and technology as the main drivers of
modernization. In particular, the increase in individual autonomy, secularization, and
female emancipation ultimately reduces fertility rates, in part through the childbearing
postponement mechanism (Sobotka 2004). While most interpretations and empirical
applications of the SDT highlight its ideation and culture component over employment
uncertainty, Kotowska et al. (2008) argue that, in the case of Poland, labor market
conditions and educational expansion (e.g., structure component of SDT) seem to
be most pronounced. While originally formulated to explain changing demographic

18 CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
patterns in developed Western societies, other work has likewise applied the SDT
components to explain trends in other contexts. Lesthaeghe (2020) uses the SDT
to explain increasing non-marital cohabitation in the Philippines, juxtaposed with
strong stigmatization of this behavior by Catholics who comprise around 80% of the
population.
AnotherphenomenonthatoccursalongsidetheSDTisglobalizationand,relatedly,
rising uncertainty in labor market positions (Blossfeld et al. 2006). As such, the
processes alongside globalization, such as wage competition and a “race-to-the-bottom”
approach, might partly explain the rise in employment uncertainty and, consequently,
fertility postponement and/or reduction (Mills and Blossfeld 2013). Apart from an
increased interconnectedness of markets, globalization likewise facilitates the flow of
ideas, which in part explains the shift in cultural norms and values referred to by
the SDT. Increased globalization and competition within labor markets necessitated
countries to implement policies geared toward educational expansion. These have
implications for fertility differentials across individuals (women, in particular) of
different levels of educational attainment. In the UK, for instance, highly educated
women have higher rates of childlessness, later ages at first childbirth, and a lower
likelihood of meeting desired family size (Berrington and Pattaro 2014; Berrington
et al. 2015).
Figure 1.4: Global Trends in Total Fertility Rates (1960-2022)
Note: IncomegroupsarebasedonthelatestclassificationbytheWorldBankusingpercapita
Gross National Income while regional groups are based on the World Bank’s administrative
divisions. Total fertility rates (TFRs) estimate the average number of children per woman if
she survives through her biological childbearing period and experiences the same age-specific
fertility rates of that year.

1.3. DEMOGRAPHIC REPERCUSSIONS OF EMPLOYMENT UNCERTAINTY19
Examining trends over time, total fertility rates have remarkably decreased over
the last 60 years. Figure 1.4 shows that high-income countries, on average, saw a
decline in fertility rates until 1990, which have plateaued since then. On the contrary,
low and lower-middle-income countries have been experiencing a pronounced decrease
in fertility rates. By regional groups, Sub-Saharan Africa has notably lagged in its
fertility decline, at least compared to the Middle East and North Africa, South Asia,
and Latin America, despite having about the same fertility rate in 1960.
1.3.2 Contemporary (Macro) Findings on Employment
Uncertainty
Each operationalization of employment uncertainty in Table 1.1 has spurred separate
strands of the literature, although some (e.g., positional and durational) are arguably
more explored than others (e.g., class-based, caregiving) (Buh 2023). A meta-analysis
of the effect of unemployment and fixed-term employment on fertility behavior in
Europe reveals that this nexus is, by and large, negative but gendered (Alderotti et al.
2021). Notably, female fixed-term employment and male unemployment are found
to negatively affect fertility, while the negative effect is most striking in Southern
European countries where social protection systems and family policies are weaker
(Alderotti et al. 2021). In each chapter of the dissertation, the literature on each type
of employment uncertainty and its individual-level effects on fertility is examined in
closer detail.
In lieu of focusing on the vast micro-level literature of employment uncertainty-
fertility which the stand-alone chapters do so in detail, this section highlights macro-
level patterns and trends. Macro (e.g., cross-country) approaches to studying the
effects of unemployment, and macroeconomic conditions more broadly, on fertility rate
have been extensively used. On one hand, fertility postponement was more prevalent
in countries with high and persistent unemployment (Adsera 2005, 2011). Apart from
postponement, unfavorable economic conditions in developed countries led to lower
total fertility (Karaman Örsal and Goldstein 2018). Indeed, most studies in this area
of research find fertility to be pro-cyclical—it typically decreases during periods of
high unemployment and increases otherwise (Cazzola et al. 2016; Goldstein et al. 2013;
Gozgor et al. 2021; Karaman Örsal and Goldstein 2018; Sobotka et al. 2011; Comolli
2017; Doepke et al. 2023).
Most of these studies model the country-level fertility rates as a function of lagged

20 CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
unemployment rate (Goldstein et al. 2013; Karaman Örsal and Goldstein 2018; Gozgor
etal.2021;Comolli2017;BellidoandMarcén2019)(oratthesub-nationallevelasdone
by (Matysiak et al. 2021a), while some papers employ multi-level methods combining
individual-level fertility and country-level unemployment (Currie et al. 2014; Adsera
2005, 2011) or descriptive analysis of changing trends and associations (Sobotka et al.
2011;Doepkeetal.2023;Cazzolaetal.2016). Thesepapersfindanegativerelationship
between unemployment and fertility rates, although each paper carefully emphasizes
heterogeneities such that the effect sizes are stronger for certain population subgroups
like women in their 30s and middle-educated women (Comolli 2017) and in certain
regions like Southern Europe (Goldstein et al. 2013).
On the other hand, fewer papers find that an increase in aggregate unemployment
has a non-significant or even positive effect on fertility. Among this strand is that
of Fernandez-Crehuet et al. (2020) that uses fractional integration and cointegration
estimation methods and finds the absence of a long-run relationship between
unemployment and fertility, as well as the absence of a short-run relationship in most
of the developed countries in the sample. Further, unemployment has also been shown
to increase fertility in England, suggesting that the substitution effect dominates the
income effect, such that the low opportunity cost of childbearing during periods of
high unemployment facilitates childbearing (Aksoy 2016). Similarly, Karaman Örsal
and Goldstein (2018) and Doepke et al. (2023) find that among developed countries
in the 70s and 80s, good economic times (e.g., high growth, low unemployment) are
associated with lower fertility, implying that the direction of this association has indeed
changed over time.
1.3.3 Extension I. Re-examining the Unemployment-Fertility
Nexus
These contemporary findings of a macro-level relationship between unemployment and
fertility suffer from two main limitations in scope and breadth. First, as it relates to
scope, all the papers cited in the preceding section cover developed country contexts,
mostly using OECD or European macro data. This constraint naturally arises because
of the lack of long-run harmonized data on employment and fertility indicators in low
and lower-middle-income countries. In effect, the trade-off that these papers faced was
to use fewer countries (narrower geographic focus) with longer periods (more years)
rather than more countries with shorter periods. While this approach strengthens the

1.3. DEMOGRAPHIC REPERCUSSIONS OF EMPLOYMENT UNCERTAINTY21
internal validity of these papers by using reliable and consistent data, it also limits our
understanding of the unemployment-fertility nexus to a very narrow group of countries.
In other words, the “macro” unemployment-fertility papers we have to date are only
macro to the extent of high-income countries. Recent work attempts to bridge this
gap test the macro-level relationship between inequality and fertility in the Global
South (Castro Torres et al. 2022), although this analysis does not cover labor market
indicators.
In light of this limitation, this chapter re-estimates the unemployment-fertility
nexus using a larger set of countries and data from the World Bank’s World
Development Indicators. As a trade-off, this dataset only contains ILO-harmonized
estimatesoftheunemploymentrateforavastmajorityofcountriessince1990,although
its fertility data dates back to 1961. As such, the unemployment-fertility regression
models are estimated using panel data of at most 187 countries and territories from
1991-2022. In comparison, while this panel is still wider (in terms of countries) and
longer (in terms of years) than the panels used in most other macro-level analyses
(Goldstein et al. 2013; Matysiak et al. 2021a; Comolli 2017; Bellido and Marcén 2019),
it is still not as long as other analyses that use data dating back to the 70s or 80s
(Karaman Örsal and Goldstein 2018; Doepke et al. 2023).
Similar to most papers, the total fertility rate is modeled as a function of
the lagged unemployment rate using fixed effects, thereby testing the hypothesis
that the unemployment rate is negatively associated with fertility. Building on
Goldstein et al. (2013); Karaman Örsal and Goldstein (2018), and Comolli (2017) by
including additional macroeconomic and demographic controls, I estimate the model
parsimoniously specified below:
F = α+γU +δD +ωM +θZ +ϵ , (1.1)
c,t c,t−1 c,t−1 c,t−1 c,t t
where γ is the coefficient of interest, representing the effect of the lagged
unemployment rate in country c in the past year on the fertility rate at year t.
Further, D and M are vectors of demographic (population, age dependency, labor force
participation, and infant mortality rates) and macroeconomic (real per capita GDP
growth, inflation, and foreign direct investment inflows) time-varying lagged controls
to capture the potentially confounding effects of the social and economic aspects of
a country. Finally, Z is a vector of region and period fixed effects, whereas t is the
composite error term.

Table 1.2 shows the results from estimating this model. Columns (1)
to (3) estimate the effect of total unemployment rate on fertility while the 6
subsequent columns replicate these models using female and male unemployment rates,
respectively. Resultsshowarobustlynegativeeffectofunemploymentonfertilityacross
all specifications, although the magnitude of the female unemployment rate is slightly
larger compared to the male unemployment rate. In my preferred specification (which
has the complete demographic and macroeconomic controls), a one percentage point
(pp) increase in the unemployment rate is associated with an average decrease in the
TFR in the following year by 0.015 units (children per woman). For the gender-
restricted measures of unemployment rates, a one pp increase in the female and male
unemployment rates is associated with an average decrease in the subsequent year’s
TFR by 0.014 and 0.011 units, respectively.
As an additional test for heterogeneity, the negative relationship between
unemployment and fertility might be different depending on whether a country is high
or low income. In Table 1.3, the analysis is replicated for the higher and lower-income
countrysamples,respectively. Theformergroupiscomposedofupper-middleandhigh-
income countries whereas the latter comprises low and lower-middle-income countries
based on the World Bank’s most recent classification. Indeed, results suggest that the
effect size for the whole sample is the same for the higher-income sample (Column 2)
but among lower-income countries, the magnitude is larger— a one pp increase in the
lagged total unemployment rate is associated with a 0.029 units average decrease in
TFR.
One of the more difficult barriers to the macro-level analysis of fertility dynamics
is the challenge of incorporating “time” (Goldstein et al. 2013) and determining the
ideal lag of unemployment or any macroeconomic indicator being used (Sobotka et al.
2011; Karaman Örsal and Goldstein 2018). First, prior empirical exercises reveal that
fertility is a persistent (or path-dependent) (Gozgor et al. 2021; Luci-Greulich and
Thévenon 2014) and non-stationary (Cazzola et al. 2016; Fernandez-Crehuet et al.
2020) measure. As such, the formal modeling of TFRs should ideally account for
the “dynamic endogeneity” problem that arises—–that is, fertility rates at time t are
directlyinfluencedbyfertilityratesint−1. OneapproachtodosoisusingArellanoand
Bond (1991)-type estimators, particularly Blundell and Bond (1998) and Windmeijer
(2005) two-step System Generalized Method of Moments (GMM) which accounts for
thedynamicendogeneityproblemthatariseswhenthelaggedfertilityratesareincluded

among the independent variables.
Incross-countrystudiesthatanalyzetherelationshipbetweeneconomicconditions
and fertility, finding suitable instrumental variables is challenging (Karaman Örsal
and Goldstein 2018), although other studies have employed a similar GMM approach
(Gozgoretal.2021;Luci-GreulichandThévenon2013,2014). Sincethetimehorizonof
our panel is shorter relative to the number of countries, GMM estimation is also suited
to address the Nickell bias (of obtaining inconsistent estimates) in “small-T, large-N”
panels. Extending equation 1.1 by controlling for two lags of the unemployment rate
to account for longer gaps for planning and conception, I estimate:
F = α+βF +γ U +γ U +δX +θZ +ϵ , (1.2)
c,t c,t−1 1 c,t−1 2 c,t−2 s,t−1 s,t t
where γ is the coefficient of interest, representing the effect of the lagged
1
unemployment rate in country c in the past year on the fertility rate at year t. The
rest of the vectors are similar to the earlier equation. More broadly, the contributions
of this analysis are two-fold. On the methods front, it employs a dynamic panel data
estimationmethodthataddressesthedynamicendogeneityproblemthatarisesbecause
of the persistent and non-stationary nature of fertility rates. Indeed, previous work has
recognized that this “more complex estimator” (Karaman Örsal and Goldstein 2018)
generates consistent and efficient estimates under a properly specified model (Gozgor
etal.2021). Onthesubstantiveandthematicfront,itcontributestothere-examination
of the unemployment-fertility nexus that incorporates both the COVID-19 pandemic
years and the full extent of the aftermath of the Global Financial Crisis.
Results in Table 1.4 reveal that the magnitudes of the main coefficient of
interest (L.Unemployment Rate) are quite similar to those in the fixed effects models,
demonstrating robustness. A one pp increase in the lagged total, female, and male
unemployment rates are associated with lower TFRs by 0.016, 0.019, and 0.012 units,
respectively. In addition, TFR is indeed persistent and non-stationary as shown by
the lagged TFR being significant. Finally, the second lag of the unemployment rate
is also robustly significant and positive, such that a one pp increase in total, female,
and male unemployment is associated with a 0.017, 0.022, and 0.012 units increase in
the TFR two years later. A plausible conjecture might be postponement— as a result
of an increase in unemployment at time t, births that would have otherwise occurred
at time t+1 just occurred at time t+2 instead, echoing the tempo-specific effects of
macroeconomic conditions described in prior studies (Sobotka et al. 2011).

1.3. DEMOGRAPHIC REPERCUSSIONS OF EMPLOYMENT UNCERTAINTY27
1.3.4 Extension II. Novel Analysis Using Other Measures of
Employment Uncertainty
The second limitation of contemporary macro-level findings summarized above, as it
relates to breadth, is their reliance on the unemployment rate, or some variation of
it (female/male/youth), as a proxy for labor market indicators. Recall that in the
schematic classification in Table 1.1, positional uncertainty (e.g., unemployment vs.
employment) is only one among many dimensions of employment uncertainty. In
most of the developing world where unemployment is persistently low (see Figure 1.1),
vulnerable employment seems to be the larger and more pressing problem (see Figure
1.2). Similarly, self-employment and part-time employment are alternative measures of
employment uncertainty. How these indicators affect fertility rates has not been tested
in the literature to date, to the best of my knowledge.
Following the exact estimation strategies in equations 1.1 and 1.2, I analyze
the effect of three alternative measures of employment uncertainty: (1) vulnerable
employment rate; (2) self-employment rate; and (3) part-time employment rate. One
limitation of the WDI data is that the ILO-harmonized part-time employment rates
cover fewer years than the other two measures. As such, the sample size is severely
lessened in these models. For conciseness, only the results of the fully specified models
are presented (including demographic and macroeconomic controls).
Results in Table 1.5 suggest a negative effect of vulnerable and self-employment.
A one pp increase in the lagged VER and SER, as a share of the total employed
population, is associated with 0.008 units of lower average TFR. Note that these two
variablescouldbetakenasindicatorsofhowlargeaproblememploymentuncertaintyis
among the employed population, a metric that the unemployment rate simply does not
account for. Further, part-time ER has a statistically insignificant effect, in part owing
to the very small sample size in that model. On the other hand, GMM estimates in
Table 1.6 do not find any of the three employment measures as significant determinants
of fertility rates, thereby suggesting caution in interpreting the findings of the fixed
effects models and warranting more and perhaps narrower analyses.
Taken together, these analyses lend evidence to the idea that (higher)
unemployment rates are a robustly strong determinant of (lower) total fertility rates,
whereas the effect of vulnerable and self-employment rates are also negative, but not
robust to alternative estimation strategies.
There are other shortcomings of the “macro” approach that warrant future work.

30 CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
First,totalfertilityratesareperiod-specificmeasures,whichmeansthatwecannotinfer
whether declining fertility rates are mainly explained by timing or quantum effects, or
a combination of both. For this, cohort-specific fertility data might be more suitable
as has been used by (Pifarré i Arolas 2017). However, this isn’t available for most
countries included in the extension analyses conducted above. Further, TFRs or crude
birth rates as used in most macro analyses are also unable to distinguish between birth
order, which means we cannot distinguish whether declining fertility rates manifest
through a reduction in first births (or increasing childlessness) or through a reduction
in higher-order births (or decreasing family size). While some countries have order-
specific fertility data (Goldstein et al. 2013), this is unavailable for most countries
around the world.
1.3.5 Country-in-Focus: Germany
Germany, like many other European countries, is undergoing profound demographic
changes characterized by sub-replacement fertility rates, an increasing share of elderly
in the population, and an increasing international migrant stock. Adapting to
these demographic changes is core to securing Germany’s economic future, as these
developments have accompanying implications for labor supply, pension fund solvency,
and healthcare systems. The literature has extensively analyzed the factors affecting
fertility, migration, and life expectancy in Germany. While changing social norms
that contribute to the postponement of family formation is a plausible explanation
of fertility trends, employment uncertainty, political events (e.g., reunification), and
policy reforms also play a role.
Among developed economies where the employment uncertainty-fertility nexus
has been studied extensively, Germany is interesting for three non-exhaustive reasons.
First, since the end of World War II, Germany never really had very high levels of
fertility rates in comparison with other developed Western economies. Whereas the
post-war period saw the TFR increase in the US up to around 3.6 in 1960, Germany’s
“peak” post-war fertility was around 2.5 in 1965 (see Figure 1.4). Compared to other
high-income countries in the world, Germany’s TFR has been consistently below the
average of its income group (see Figure 1.4). Germany’s TFR reached the replacement
level of 2.1 in the early 70s and has remained in sub-replacement levels ever since.1
1Note that these observations are based on WDI data that combine East and West Germany, two
countries with notably different fertility rates and patterns. See Goldstein and Kreyenfeld (2011).

1.3. DEMOGRAPHIC REPERCUSSIONS OF EMPLOYMENT UNCERTAINTY31
Figure 1.5: Total Fertility Rates in Select Developed Countries (1960-2022)
Second, in recent decades, it is interesting how Germany’s fertility rates have hovered
much closer to those of Southern European countries like Spain and Italy, despite
having substantially different welfare regimes. Figure 1.4 shows that Germany only
broke apart from Spain and Italy’s fertility levels in the early 2010s. Finally, it is of
course notable that Germany was divided into East and West for around 4 decades,
with both countries having distinct fertility rates as shown below.
Looking at period fertility rates is instructive in that it offers a comprehensive
snapshot of childbearing in a country but it is also limited as it does not reveal the
age distribution of mothers, which varies over time. Figure 1.6 shows the age-specific
fertility rates (ASFR) in East and West Germany in 1956 and 2016, a 60-year gap
with the reunification occurring in the middle (1990). As the TFR is simply a sum
of the ASFRs, the plot shows the age composition of TFR by region between these
two periods. Notably, the peak of both curves has gradually shifted rightward over
time, implying postponement from about the early 20s in 1956 to around 30 in 2016.
Further, the area of the curves has also shrunk considerably (implying lower TFR).
While East and West German fertility rates have arguably somehow converged, there

32 CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
Figure 1.6: Age-Specific Fertility Rates in Germany: A 60-year Comparison
still seems to be a slight difference, especially in terms of the median age of first birth.
These interesting patterns and developments of fertility in Germany warrant a
closer examination. In Chapter 3, fertility is analyzed using the event and timing of
childbearing within 10 years after entering the labor market. In Chapter 4, fertility
is operationalized through higher-order births (i.e., having second and third births).
Finally, Chapter 5 measures fertility intentions, arguably a precondition of fertility
behavior.
1.4 Methodological Approach and Summary of
Chapters
1.4.1 Causal Identification
A common thread of these four stand-alone chapters is the application of both
established and emerging causal identification strategies in quantitative social science

1.4. METHODOLOGICAL APPROACH AND SUMMARY OF CHAPTERS 33
research. The “causality revolution”, where closer attention is given to establishing
causal relationships, emerged as researchers developed novel statistical techniques
and used larger and more comprehensive datasets that mimic experimental designs
as closely as possible. A core tenet of this set of methodological tools is the
use of counterfactuals and potential outcomes (Rubin 2005; Winship and Morgan
1999), which involves comparing observed outcomes to what would have happened
under different conditions. Where randomized control trials (RCTs) are not feasible
or ethical, quasi-experimental methods leverage on approximations of experimental
conditions typically using observational data. Survey experiments, where certain
survey components are randomized across respondents, have likewise emerged as a
novel methodological tool to estimate causal effects.
These identification strategies are common in economics and, increasingly, in
political science and sociology (Gangl 2010). The use of quasi-experimental strategies
also seems to be fitting for questions that are of interest to demographers. For instance,
at the individual, how does the experience of unemployment at time t affect a person’s
fertility decisions at time t + n? Or at the macro level, how does a certain event
(e.g., COVID-19 pandemic) affect overall fertility rates? Bhrolcháin and Dyson (2007)
make an interesting point—in 1966, the year of the Fire Horse, crude birth rates
in Japan fell by 25% compared to the previous year owing to the folk belief of bad
luck for childbearing. Without resorting to any advanced quasi-experimental models,
observants describe this phenomenon as causal, using arguments that are comparable
to the intuition of formal models—it is pronounced, temporary, proportional, and non-
recurrent (Bhrolcháin and Dyson 2007). The same argument can be made in other
notable “kinks” in demographic patterns—in contemporary China, annual population
growth rates were negative in 1960 and 1961, directly as a consequence of the Great
Chinese Famine emanating from the Great Leap Forward campaign. In these cases,
demographers were able to intuitively argue the underlying causal mechanisms of a
demographic event. Beyond intuition, there is an increasing use of causal identification
strategies in demographic research (Huinink and Brüderl 2021; Engelhardt et al. 2009;
Wunsch and Gourbin 2020; Kreyenfeld 2021; Mize and Manago 2022), some of which
are employed in this dissertation— namely, difference-in-differences (DiD), matching,
and survey experiment designs.
These methods are employed on two main data sources. For Chapter 2, merged
quarterly Labor Force Surveys (LFS) of the Philippines from 2016 to 2020 are used.

| 34  |     |     |     | CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | ----------------------- |
The LFS is a quarterly survey of around 40,000 households that is nationally and
regionally representative. Absent register and panel data, the LFS is the most widely
used data source for most countries for studying labor markets. For Chapters 3, 4, the
German Socio-Economic Panel (GSOEP) (Goebel et al. 2019b) is used. It has been
an annual household panel since 1984 and is a widely-used longitudinal data source,
comparable to the Panel Survey of Income Dynamics (PSID) in the US and the UK
British Household Panel Survey (BHPS), among others. In Chapter 5, we develop a
novel survey experiment and fielded it in the GSOEP’s Innovation Sample (GSOEP-
IS), a subsample of the main GSOEP intended for the implementation of innovative
| and/or experimental | survey      | instruments. |           |              |
| ------------------- | ----------- | ------------ | --------- | ------------ |
| 1.4.2 Summary       | of          | Chapters     |           |              |
|                     | Figure 1.7: | Dissertation | Framework | and Chapters |
Figure 1.7 presents the overarching substantive framework of this dissertation,
where employment uncertainty is central and is depicted as both a determined and a
deterministic phenomenon. It is determined in the sense that it is in part a function of
periods of economic uncertainty, broadly defined, including recessions, pandemics, and
their accompanying policies. While it is deterministic of a broad set of life course

outcomes, this dissertation specifically focuses on fertility behavior and intentions

uncertainty and intervening state policies is unpacked in the case of the Philippines.
Leveraging on the regional variation in the imposition of extreme and militarized
lockdowns at the onset of the COVID-19 pandemic, this chapter employs a two-way
fixed effects DiD design to analyze its effects on the probability of being informally
employed. Previewing the results, the article finds that the lockdowns increased
the probability of informal employment for women but not for men, and is most
pronounced among mothers with minor children. This informalization phenomenon
is demonstrated to have compositional and survivalist explanations and highlights the
| dual | role that | women play | as           | workers | and     | carers of first resort. |     |             |     |
| ---- | --------- | ---------- | ------------ | ------- | ------- | ----------------------- | --- | ----------- | --- |
|      | In        | 3, the     | relationship |         | between |                         |     |             |     |
|      | Chapter   |            |              |         |         | initial employment      |     | uncertainty | and |
subsequent fertility, a subject that has received extensive attention in the literature,
is revisited using a twostep identification strategy in Germany. Operationalizing
initial uncertain employment conditions using durational (fixed-term employment) and
recessionary (recession year entry) typologies (see Section 1.1), the article examines
their effect on first birth hazards within ten years after entry. Using the German
Socio-Economic Panel (GSOEP), the article employs a two-step combination of
propensity score matching and survival analysis, a method that has been used in the
epidemiological literature, to address the issues of selection into first jobs. Previewing
the results, fixed-term employment upon entry is demonstrated to negatively affect
subsequent fertility for women but not for men, whereas recession entry negatively
| affects | fertility | for men. |              |     |         |                    |     |             |     |
| ------- | --------- | -------- | ------------ | --- | ------- | ------------------ | --- | ----------- | --- |
|         | In        | 4, the   | relationship |     | between |                    |     |             |     |
|         | Chapter   |          |              |     |         | current employment |     | uncertainty | and |
fertility behavior is examined. Operationalizing employment uncertainty using a class-
based lens, the article distinguishes the social classes of men and women based on
their occupational categories into upper service, lower service, skilled manual/higher-
grade routine nonmanual, and semi-/unskilled manual/lower-grade routine nonmanual
classes. Using the GSOEP, the article uses survival analysis methods to unpack
the relationship between social class and higher-order births. The focus on higher-
order births is warranted in that it takes a certain amount of time for individuals to
reach a certain level of “occupational maturity”. In their seminal work, Erikson et al.
(1979a, 1983) uses people’s occupational positions in mid-30s to construct social class

schemas. Thus, thisworkspeakstothestrandofliteratureexaminingclassdifferentials
in completed fertility (Neyer et al. 2017; Dribe and Smith 2021) and higher-order
births (Baizan 2021; Ekert-Jaffé et al. 2002) by focusing on the case of Germany and

elevated second birth rates of individuals in service classes and the role that upward
career mobility after first birth plays in subsequent fertility decisions.
In Chapter 5, the effect of future employment uncertainty and caregiving

in Germany. This article adopts a “future narrative” framework (Vignoli et al. 2020a),
