ABSTRACT
The adverse effects of COVID-19 on labour market outcomes are amplified by
and partly attributable to the imposition of extreme mobility restrictions. While
gendered disparities in job losses and working hours reduction are demonstrated in
the literature, is an informalization of employment observed, and is this phenomenon
likewise gendered? This article analyses the Philippines, a country that imposed one
of the world’s longest and strictest lockdowns, specifically how its imposition affected
informal employment. A conceptual and empirical distinction between compositional
and survivalist informalization is proffered—the former referring to informality
induced by changes in the size and composition of overall employment, and the latter
on informality induced by the need to work absent sufficient welfare support and
precautionary household savings. Examining the regional variation in lockdowns as a
quasi-experiment, results demonstrate that extreme lockdowns increase the probability
| of informal | employment | among employed | women | but not for | men. |
| ----------- | ---------- | -------------- | ----- | ----------- | ---- |
Keywords: COVID-19, gender, informal employment, survivalist motives,
| compositional | factors |     |     |     |     |
| ------------- | ------- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
39

40 CHAPTER 2. LOCKDOWNS AND INFORMALITY
2.1 Introduction
The COVID-19 pandemic is unique in that broad and stringent mobility restrictions,
which were not present in previous crises, were imposed. While general labour
market outcomes (e.g. employment, labour force participation) have been analyzed
in the growing strand of the literature on the effects of COVID, the impact of
extreme lockdowns on employment, independent of the pandemic’s blanket economic
effects, have received little attention. Indeed, the pandemic caused what can
be considered a “She-cession”—job losses disproportionately affected women across
geographical and socio-economic contexts. In Latin America, 56 percent of job losses
involved women and in most low and lower-middle income countries, working hours
decreased disproportionately more for women (Cucagna and Romero 2021; Maurizio
2021). Among developed countries, job loss and decrease in working hours were
disproportionately higher among mothers, at least in part due to their increased care
responsibilities (Del Boca et al. 2020; Dias et al. 2020; Qian and Hu 2021). In many
developing countries where informal employment constitutes a sizeable fraction of the
employed population, informalization as a result of COVID-19 is thus far understudied
(Jaga and Ollier-Malaterre 2022).
Lockdowns were common at the onset of the pandemic. Absent sufficient
information about the transmission channels and mortality incidence of the disease,
many countries erred on the side of caution and imposed extreme mobility restrictions
thatwereinstrumentalintaminginitialCOVIDoutbreaks(PajaronandVasquez2023).
However,whatexactlyisrestrictedandhowmuchtheserestrictionsareenforcedlargely
vary across countries. This article looks into the Philippines—widely reported by
international media organizations as having imposed one of the world’s longest and
strictest lockdowns, with severe mobility restrictions enforced by military and police
forces (Reuters 2020; Olanday and Rigby 2020; See 2021). In lockdown areas, all
forms of public transport were stopped, offices and schools were entirely closed, and
recreational activities, such as going for a walk, have also been disallowed. As an
archipelagic country, not all regions in the Philippines were affected by COVID at the
same time and with the same severity. Thus, widespread regional lockdowns, solely
determined by the national government, applied only to some regions, whereas others
underwent less restrictive containment measures. The labour market implications of
this spatial variation in the design and enforcement of lockdown policies are thus far
barely explored.

2.1. INTRODUCTION 41
Among many labour market phenomena, “informalization” is particularly relevant
for two reasons, apart from that it constitutes the majority of the globally employed
population. First, informal workers generally experience greater precarity and
uncertainty, leading to worse financial satisfaction and overall well-being (Ferrer-I-
Carbonell and Gërxhani 2011; Webb et al. 2020).. Second, economic response (e.g.
“pump-prime”) policies during periods of crises may have unwarranted consequences on
labour market formalization. State deregulation succeeding the Global Financial Crisis
is positively associated with the size of the informal economy and is partly responsible
for its growth in some countries (Kus 2010). Despite COVID-19 lockdowns directly
affecting an estimated 1.1 billion informally employed workers heavily concentrated in
vulnerable and developing countries (ILO 2020), our understanding of informalization
remains largely descriptive, with little evidence of its underlying determinants.
Looking back at previous economic downturns in developing countries is
suggestive; there is some consensus that informal employment is countercyclical
(Albertinietal.2019;Colomboetal.2019). Immediatelyafterperiodsofbankingcrises
in Mexico, Brazil, Costa Rica, and Bolivia in 1994, as well as after the Argentinian peso
crisis of 2001 and its 2002 spillover crisis in Uruguay, informal employment increased
and remained persistently high for succeeding years (Colombo et al. 2019). The same
is true after natural disasters (Mendoza and Jara 2020; Pecha Garzón 2017).
However, little is said as to why we observe such informalization and this
article conceptually and empirically fills this gap by distinguishing two channels.
First, compositional factors, namely the size and composition of overall employment,
shapes informal employment rates. When overall employment decreases and formal
decreases more than informal does, this results in a “mechanical” increase in informal
employment. Netofthese“compositionaleffects”, however, theinformalsectorhaslong
been pointed in the literature to play a cushioning role especially in developing country
contexts. When economic uncertainty is high and formal job opportunities are scarce,
workers would rather work informally rather than be unemployed or inactive. More
broadly, the informal sector becomes part of one’s “survival strategy” in the absence of
formal employment opportunities and government support in times of need Macdonald
(1994).
Regarding gendered differences, women are disproportionately more likely to
work informally during and after recessions. First, there are generally more limited
opportunities for older and lower-educated women in the formal sector, which are

42 CHAPTER 2. LOCKDOWNS AND INFORMALITY
even more scarce during downturns (Floro et al. 2009; McCaig and Pavcnik 2015).
Second, the flexibility of working conditions in informal employment allow women,
who bear the brunt of care work, to better balance work and family responsibilities
(Finnegan and Singh 2004; Rodin et al. 2012)). Indeed, during and after the 1997
Asian Financial Crisis, it was mostly older women in South Korea who were pushed
intoinformalemploymentsinceprevailinggenderandfamilynormsconcentratedformal
workopportunitiestomenandyoungerwomen(Floroetal.2009). Still, eveninnormal
times,informalemploymentindevelopingcountriesispredominantlyfemale-dominated
(ILO 2018).
In the earlier months of 2020, it was not only the severity and contagiousness of
COVID that was uncertain but also the length and tightness of ensuing lockdowns.
Lockdowns triggered larger disruptions in economic activity—the length (Coccia 2021)
and stringency (König and Winkler 2021) of lockdowns have a negative effect on
aggregate economic output. To contribute to a broader understanding of the labour
market effects of lockdowns in countries with sizable informal sectors, this article
analyses whether extreme lockdowns increase the probability of being informally
employed and whether this effect varies by gender. Using 16 pooled quarterly Labour
Force Survey (LFS) data from the Philippines since 2016, the article employs a two-
way fixed effects difference-in-differences (TWFE DD) approach and compares the
probability of informal employment in lockdown and non-lockdown regions before and
during the lockdown.
The article’s contributions are three-fold. First, it makes a conceptual and
empirical distinction between compositional and survivalist factors as the underlying
causes of employment informalization during the lockdown—a distinction that also
applies to explaining informal employment dynamics in general. Second, it fills
the gap in the literature on how state actions during crises enable gendered
informalization (Hammer and Ness 2021) by isolating the “lockdown effect” from the
other “recessionary” effects of the pandemic. Finally, this article broadly speaks to
the literature on how informally employed women bear the brunt of labour market
consequences during periods of crisis and how women play a dual role as both workers
and carers of first resort.

2.2. LITERATURE REVIEW 43
2.2 Literature Review
Expansive and stringent lockdowns were periods of increased social and economic
uncertainty that were inherently different from the uncertainty brought about by
previous financial crises or recurrent natural disasters and weather shocks. There
was unpredictability not only in terms of the contagiousness and severity of COVID
but also in the length and stringency of extreme lockdowns. This section elaborates
our expectations of the patterns of informalization and its gendered dimensions. In so
doing, a conceptual distinction is made between compositional factors and survivalist
motives that drive the informalization process (Kalleberg and Vallas 2017; Lee et al.
2020; Rogan et al. 2017).
Compositional factors encompass movements in the size and composition of
overall employment—a reflection of the labour and capital structure where large
swaths of the employed population are constrained in informal arrangements given
low absorption in and intense competition for formal jobs. Survivalist motives on
the other hand refer to the financial security that one gains from participating in the
“shadow economy”, which encompasses informal work, rather than being unemployed
orinactive. Unsurprisingly, inmanymacro-levelanalyses, informalemploymentfollows
a countercyclical pattern—it increases when economic output falls as it absorbs some
people who lose jobs in the formal sector (Colombo et al. 2019; Feridhanusetyawan
and Gaduh 2000; Fernández and Meza 2015; Floro and Dymski 2000; Nguyen and Su
2021).
The idea that the probability of working informally rises as a result of lockdowns
cannot be simply taken as given, considering that the size, nature, and regulations
governing informal work vary across developing countries. Contrary to earlier findings,
Maurizio et al. (2023) demonstrate that during the COVID-induced contraction phase
insixLatinAmericancountries,informalemploymentexacerbatedthedeclineinoverall
employment and did not play its usual countercyclical role. Further investigations in
other contexts, as well as those that make a more nuanced distinction between the
effects of lockdowns from that of COVID, would offer a more refined take on this idea
including why informal employment responds as such.

44 CHAPTER 2. LOCKDOWNS AND INFORMALITY
2.2.1 How Extreme Lockdowns “Informalize” Employment
There are multiple channels through which extreme lockdowns may affect
informal employment including both compositional factors (macro) and survivalist
motives (micro-level behaviour). On aggregate, changes between employment and
unemployment as well as formal and informal work affect the overall size and
composition of the workforce. It is reasonable to expect that stronger lockdowns, likely
due to higher incidence of COVID-19, induce larger contractions of overall employment
(Qian and Fan 2020).
Suppose that due to an exogenous shock, the formal sector is more adversely hit
than the informal sector. In this case, the decrease in formal employment outweighs
the decrease in informal employment such that informal employment (as a share of
the workforce) increases1 even if the number of informally employed people does not
necessarily increase. One example is the case of Latin American countries where
lockdowns amplified the decrease in regular employment in terms of working hours
and actual number of jobs, but did not have the same effect on informal employment
(Maurizio 2021).
Since lockdowns impose mobility restrictions, those who are working home-based
informal jobs such as piece-rate and freelance workers may find it easier to retain jobs
rather than formal workers whose jobs require physical presence (e.g. retail services,
hospitality and tourism, etc.) (ILO 2020; Maurizio 2021). Meanwhile, self-employed
individuals who typically rely on working outdoors (e.g. street vendors) face the risk
of arrest if caught outside since these activities are prohibited during a lockdown.
This “chilling effect” of extreme lockdowns compositionally increases the probability
of working informally if employment losses are more concentrated among the formally
employed.
On the other hand, involuntarily displaced workers in the Philippines face
a massive constraint—social assistance mechanisms are largely absent and savings
are low. Under these conditions, one cannot afford to be unemployed and would
rather work informally, a mechanism classically referred to in the sociology literature
as survivalist motive (Macdonald 1994; Lee et al. 2020) and in the economics
literature as the “luxury unemployment hypothesis” (Udall and Sinclair 1982). Indeed,
1Consider a simple computation of informal employment rates (IER) as follows: IER=I/(F+I)
where I and F denote the number of informally and formally employed individuals respectively, such
that I=1 and F=2 yields an informal employment rate of 0.33. Suppose that F decreases to 1.5 and I
remains at 1, such that the decrease in F is larger than the decrease in I, IER increases to 0.4.

2.2. LITERATURE REVIEW 45
widely-accessible insurance programs are absent in the Philippines, although some
institutionalized assistance programs are present.2 While there was a one-time cash
transferforpoorerhouseholdsattheonsetofthepandemic,thiswasplaguedbydelayed
distribution (Cervantes, 2020) and a relatively low payout.3 Ordinary households are
also beset by having insufficient savings due to low average savings rate ( 5%) of
disposable income (Terada-Hagiwara 2009)).
The inability of many households to weather episodes of involuntary job loss in
developing countries means that workers in low-income households cannot afford to be
unemployed and would rather take a job even if conditions are poor or informal. The
survivalist motive also captures the flexibility advantage of informal work—since these
are usually occupations with no fixed hours, workers can plan around a schedule that
allows for balancing work and family responsibilities (Sarker 2021; Singh and Kaur
2022). Figure 4.1 summarizes both compositional and survivalist informalization—two
non-mutually exclusive mechanisms of employment informalization. In light of these
factors, our prior expectations point to a lockdown-induced informalization warranting
a test of this hypothesis:
H1: Extreme lockdowns increase the probability of informal employment, conditional
on being employed.

displacedmigrantworkers. TheDepartmentofTradeandIndustry(DTI)LivelihoodSeedingProgram

of 8,000 pesos (U˜SD 140), which is slightly below the national poverty line income of 8,022 pesos.

2.2.2 How "Informalization" is a Gendered Phenomenon
The literature on the COVID-19 “She-cession” demonstrates the gendered effects of
the pandemic across geographical and socio-economic settings. In both developed and
developing countries, job losses and decreases in working hours were disproportionately
higher for women (Cucagna and Romero 2021; Del Boca et al. 2020; Dias et al. 2020;
Fan and Moen 2022; Maurizio 2021; Yavorsky et al. 2021; Ducanes and Ramos 2023)..
Among the most prominent explanations is that despite the increased involvement
of fathers in housework during the pandemic, increased care responsibilities were
disproportionatelyshoulderedbymothers(Collinsetal.2021;CraigandChurchill2021;
Kreyenfeld and Zinn 2021; Yaish et al. 2021). Household responsibilities are performed
predominantly by women in the Philippines (Bayudan-Dacuycuy and Dacuycuy 2017).
Informal employment and gender are closely intertwined subjects in developing
countries: in Sub-Saharan Africa, 89.7% of employed women are informal, and in Latin
America, Southeast Asia, and South Asia, informal employment rates are higher for
women (ILO 2018). Rodin et al. (2012) find that the majority of informally employed
women are involuntary and constrained by family responsibilities—they would have

2.2. LITERATURE REVIEW 47
preferred a more stable formal job had there been an opportunity. In the Philippines,
Gaerlan (2011) finds that the flexible nature of informal work for female garment
workers allows them to combine multiple sources of income since one job alone cannot
sufficiently pay for the entirety of expenses.
Sinceinformalemploymenthaslessbarrierstoentry, informalworkerswouldlikely
want to continue working at the height of the lockdowns, especially in the absence of
social protection mechanisms and with grimmer opportunities in the formal sector.
Ogando et al. (2021) find that in twelve cities with large rates of informality, the paid
and unpaid work that informally employed women did during the pandemic served
as “shock absorbers” and coping mechanisms. Broadly, this survivalist motive among
informally employed women is both an “exercise of agency to improve living conditions”
(Lee et al. 2020) and a manifestation of their dual role as workers and carers of first
resort. During uncertain times when there are greater threats to economic survival,
women with the survivalist motive in mind simultaneously perform paid and unpaid
work rather than trading off one for the other.
The informalization of women’s work when the economy is dire is not unfounded.
Floro and Meurs (2009) show that female informal employment rates in South Korea
increased after the 1997 Asian Financial Crisis as formal job opportunities were
concentratedonmen. Thiseffectispronouncedforolderwomensincemenandyounger
womentendtoacceptlowerwages. Asimilarincreaseinwomen’sinformalemployment
was found in Indonesia, Mexico, and Argentina during their respective episodes of
financial crisis (Albertini et al. 2019; Feridhanusetyawan and Gaduh 2000; Fernández
and Meza 2015). In terms of non-financial crises, weather shocks, which are common
in many Asia-Pacific countries including the Philippines, resulted to reduced wages
and salaries for informal workers and lower income for the informally self-employed
(Bayudan-Dacuycuy and Baje 2019). The probability of being in informal employment
rises in Ecuador after local earthquakes (Mendoza and Jara 2020) and in Jamaica after
hurricanes (Pecha Garzón 2017).
However, informalization could also similarly be more pronounced among males
when considering a traditional male-oriented breadwinner model in the Philippines
(David 1994) where males assume the sole responsibility of paid work whereas females
assume unpaid care work. From a compositional perspective, if the shift from formal
to informal male employment is larger in lockdown than non-lockdown regions, this
would also induce informalization. Thus, it cannot be presumed or taken for granted

48 CHAPTER 2. LOCKDOWNS AND INFORMALITY
that informalization is a women-only phenomenon, although existing prior evidence
largely points to this direction. Thus, we test the hypothesis that:
H2: Extreme lockdowns increase the probability of informal employment more for
women than men.
2.3 Data and Identification Strategy
2.3.1 Labour Force Survey
The Labour Force Survey (LFS) is a quarterly survey of around 40,000 households,
representative at the national and regional levels in the Philippines. Absent
administrative and panel data, the LFS is the most widely used data for many
developing countries in labour market research. To sufficiently establish the trends
in informal employment before the imposition of the extreme lockdown, the article
uses quarterly LFS data since 2016 to restrict the sample to the then-incumbent
administration that assumed office in June 2016.
The analysis is likewise restricted to regions that underwent a stringently imposed
regional lockdown (“treatment”) and those that did not impose any lockdowns at the
regional level in favour of more targeted ones (“control”). Many regions changed the
lockdown status on 01 May 2020 and since the second quarter of 2020 round of the
LFS was conducted from 16 April to 08 May 2020, only regions that had a consistent
classification throughout the survey period were retained in the sample.4
It is important to establish that the “treatment” (lockdown) is similar and
comparable across all regions that were under regional lockdowns. Similarly, in the
counterfactual analysis, the control group (no lockdown) does not imply the absence
of any mobility restrictions but rather the absence of a regionally-imposed extreme
lockdown. Since the COVID-19 response in the Philippines at the onset is centralized,
the design and implementation of these regulations are uniform across all lockdown
4Thisrestrictionintheanalysisarisesduetotheabsenceofdataonthedateoftheexactinterview
of the respondent. All respondents who were in full/no lockdown regions in April but not in May
weredroppedfromthesampletofullyobservetheeffectofthelockdownasatreatment. InAppendix
Figure B.1 I show the timeline of the lockdowns in the Philippines by region. After 15 May 2020, all
regions underwent more granular semi-lockdowns—these were less restrictive and targeted lockdowns
in smaller administrative units (i.e. cities) within each region. Therefore, periods after the second
quarterof2020areexcludedsincelockdownswerenolongerregionallyimposedandtheLFSdatahas
larger margins of error for sub-regional analyses.

the pandemic (April-May 2020).

One important distinction between the lockdown and non-lockdown regions is that
police and military enforcement is heightened and concentrated in lockdown regions.
Hapal (2021) argues that the government’s approach at the onset of the pandemic
is comparable to “waging a war” but since “COVID is an unseen enemy”, policing
was targeted towards violators of COVID restrictions. To show that this translated to
differentmobilitychangesbetweenlockdownandnon-lockdownregions,weverify,using
Google Community Mobility Reports Google (2021), that there was a larger average
mobility reduction in lockdown regions during the conduct of the April 2020 round of
the LFS. Figure B.2 also shows that the gap between the lockdown and non-lockdown
regions dissipated over time as restrictions became spatially granular and targeted.
2.3.2 Measurement and Analytical Sample
Given that the LFS is typically limited in capturing informal employment, the article
reliesontechnicalguidancefromtheILOonmeasuringinformalemploymentfromLFS
microdata (Simons and Lake 2006). From a gendered perspective, household surveys
areshowntodisproportionatelyunderestimatewomen’slabourmarketparticipation,as
theytendtoundercountintermittent,home-based,anduncompensatedwork(Langsten
and Salen 2008). Despite these limitations, LFS remains a widely used data to
analyselabourmarketdynamics,includingthatofinformalemployment(Cabegin2008;
Comola and De Mello 2011).
Consistent with ILO’s guidelines, informal employment in this article encompasses
non-professional own-account workers and contributing family workers (in their own

50 CHAPTER 2. LOCKDOWNS AND INFORMALITY
or another household) outside the agricultural sector and public sector. The “non-
professional” exclusion restriction prevents self-practicing or freelancing professionals
such as lawyers and accountants from being included in the definition. This type of
self-employment, where invoices are typically issued and thus covered by taxation, is
not informal by definition and thus makes the exclusion of professionals a reasonable
restriction.
The agriculture sector is likewise peculiar because there is no consensus on what
informality in this sector looks like, as confirmed by the International Conference of
Labour Statisticians (ICLS) (Hussmanns 2004). For instance, an own-account worker
who is an employee of a trading agricultural cooperative is considered in informal
employment if the said cooperative is an informal enterprise, which is loosely defined
in the agriculture sector (ASEAN and Ducanes 2019; Vanek et al. 2014). Finally, the
public sector is also excluded because the nature of employment here is largely formal.
These three exclusions are consistent with ILO recommendations and practices.
Beyond these formal measures, what does informal employment actually look
like in the middle of the pandemic? In the definition we adopt, non-professional
self-employed individuals are classified as informally employed. This includes street
vendors, non-registered online sellers, and domestic care workers, among others, some
of who are able to keep their jobs during the lockdown. Those who were laid off may
have also done informal online selling as an income-generating means of survival.5
Consistent with official definitions, the sample is likewise restricted to individuals
15 years old and above, also considering that there is no retirement age in the informal
sector and informal old-age employment is prevalent in the Philippines at 52 percent
(Racelisetal.2012). Thesampleisalsorestrictedtoemploymentwithpositiveworking
hours since at the height of the pandemic, some employed individuals whose basis of
pay is daily or weekly, were not asked to report to work and had no income. To
the extent that employed workers don’t work and have no income, employment status
alone, without regard for actual work hours, becomes a poor indicator of labour market
activity. Thefinalanalyticalsampleconsistsof525,488individualsover16quartersand
supplementary analyses demonstrate the robustness of results when these restrictions
are relaxed.
5We illustrate these typical labour market transitions in Appendix Figure B.3

2.3. DATA AND IDENTIFICATION STRATEGY 51
2.3.3 Identification Strategy
The presence of a uniform widespread lockdown in select regions and its absence in
others make a differences-in-differences (DD) strategy suitable in determining the
causal effect of extreme lockdowns on informal employment. Given non-random
treatment assignment, DD can also be applied to repeated cross-sections assuming
that compositional changes do not affect the variables of interest Cunningham (2021).
The key identifying assumption is that of parallel trends—the outcome variables for
both lockdown and non-lockdown regions must have been moving in a parallel manner
before the lockdown. The empirical challenge arises from the inability to observe what
informal employment in a region would have been had it not undergone a lockdown.
Empirically, placebo regressions showing whether a “lockdown effect” was present prior
to the lockdown itself can be used to demonstrate parallel trends.
Using individual-level data from the LFS, the effect of extreme lockdowns on
informal employment is estimated using a linear probability model, parsimoniously
specified as follows:
n
(cid:88)
Y = α+βLA +γLP +δ (LA ∗LP )+ θ X +ϵ , (2.1)
igt g t DD g t j jigt igt
j=1
where Y denotes informal employment, and LA and LP are binary variables
indicating if the person was surveyed in a Lockdown Area and during the Lockdown
Period, respectively. The coefficient of interest is δ which captures the causal
DD
effect of extreme lockdowns on the probability of informal employment. In the full
specification, X is a vector of individual-level j controls such as female dummy, age, and
educational level. To further analyse gendered differences, DD estimates for the male-
and female-only sample are presented. A limitation of employing the canonical 2x2
DD set-up is that there are multiple pre-treatment periods and multiple regions within
each lockdown classification. More specifically, there are 15 pre-treatment periods, 3
lockdown, and 5 non-lockdown regions. Since there are only a few regions that count
as either treated or control, clustering standard errors at the regional level introduces
the problem of “too few clusters” and, consequently, small-sample bias (Cameron and
Miller 2015). Aggregation techniques that correct such limitations, such as aggregating
pre-treatment periods into one, have relatively low statistical power (Bertrand et al.
2004). Despite this limitation, the specification below accounts for both unobserved
between-region heterogeneity and region-invariant period effects by including regional

The two-way fixed effects estimator is used in DD setups to account for
unobservable time-invariant heterogeneity as well as a group-invariant time effect
(de Chaisemartin and D’Haultfœuille 2020; Pajaron and Vasquez 2023; Wooldridge
2021). This is likely present in the setup—where unobserved sociodemographic,
economic, and political variables may vary between lockdown and non-lockdown
regions.
| 2.4   |     | Results       | and | Discussion |     |               |     |        |     |     |
| ----- | --- | ------------- | --- | ---------- | --- | ------------- | --- | ------ | --- | --- |
| 2.4.1 |     | Compositional |     | Changes    |     | in the Labour |     | Market |     |     |
Recognizing that the lockdown has affected the labour market at large, this section
contextualizes the changes within the employed population. During the lockdown
quarter, the Philippines experienced a 21 percent drop in overall paid employment
compared to the same quarter in the previous year, and these employment losses
are most pronounced in the entertainment (-50%), hospitality (-36%), and energy (-
36%) sectors (Ducanes et al. 2021). Disaggregated changes in total employment based
on lockdown status and gender show that, from a macro perspective, compositional
changes in the labour market partly explain informal employment dynamics as what
| was | theoretically |     | established | in  | Section | 2.2. |     |     |     |     |
| --- | ------------- | --- | ----------- | --- | ------- | ---- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
Figure 3.2 presents employment trends and compositional changes by lockdown
status and gender and shows that total employment decreased during the lockdown
period and the decline is more pronounced in lockdown areas. Disaggregating by
gender, total employment had a steeper decline for workers in lockdown regions, with
males being slightly more affected than females. This descriptive figure alone shows
| the | stark | and | gendered impact | of  | lockdowns | on employment. |     |     |     |     |
| --- | ----- | --- | --------------- | --- | --------- | -------------- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
However, the decrease in total employment is only one part of explaining
compositional labour market changes. In Figure 4.1, compositional informalization
occurs when the decrease in formal outweighs the decrease in informally employed

workers. Disaggregating employment changes by type and lockdown status, Table 2.2
shows two prominent findings: (1) indeed, the overall decrease in formal outweighs
that of informal employment; and (2) the decrease in formal outweighs the decrease in
| informal | employment |     | in non-lockdown |     |     | areas. |     |     |     |
| -------- | ---------- | --- | --------------- | --- | --- | ------ | --- | --- | --- |
These compositional changes are not entirely surprising. Since lockdown areas are
characterized by more severe mobility restrictions, their decline in total employment
is inevitably larger. Regarding gendered differences, the larger decline of informally
than formally employed females reflects the fact that traditionally female-dominated
informal sectors, such as retail and other service work had been more restricted during
the lockdown (Ducanes and Ramos 2023). Therefore, any gendered differences in
informal employment are at least in part due to the gendered differences on who left
| and who | stayed | in  | the labour | market |     | during the | lockdown.6 |     |     |
| ------- | ------ | --- | ---------- | ------ | --- | ---------- | ---------- | --- | --- |
Focusing specifically on the employed population, Figure 3.3 shows estimates of
informal employment, as a share of total employment, over time. From mid-2016
to January 2020, informal employment rates for lockdown and non-lockdown regions
moved in similar trends and, for the most part, in parallel. Informal employment rates
steeply increased in lockdown areas during the imposition of the extreme lockdown.
Disaggregating by gender, the trends confirm a common finding in the literature that

in most developing countries, women have a higher share in informal employment
(Cucagna and Romero 2021; Floro and Meurs 2009; Horn 2010; Gaerlan et al. 2011).
During the imposition of the extreme lockdown, it was women in lockdown regions
who experienced the steepest increase in informal employment rates, providing first-
| pass descriptive |     | evidence |     | of a | gendered | informalization. |     |     |     |     |
| ---------------- | --- | -------- | --- | ---- | -------- | ---------------- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
Figure 3.3 shows that the trends of lockdown and non-lockdown regions moved in
parallel for the most part. While this is a “first pass” test of whether the parallel trends
assumption of DD is met, additional placebo regressions are also conducted to check

2.4. RESULTS AND DISCUSSION 55
((a)) by lockdown status ((b)) by lockdown status and gender
Figure 2.3: Quarterly Informal Employment, as a share of the employed population,
2016Q3-2020Q2
whether a “lockdown effect” existed before the lockdown itself, which would invalidate
our key identifying assumption. These placebo regressions are extensively used as
sensitivity analysis and additional evidence for parallel trends in other DD studies
(Clarke and Schythe 2021; Correia 2017; Lechner 2010). Results of these analyses in
Figure 3.4 show that pre-lockdown treatment effects are significantly close to zero for
the most part, except for three quarters in 2018. Similar patterns also hold true by
gender. These regressions include the relevant covariates in equation (2), including
region and period FEs.
Broadly, other policy-related and structural factors not accounted for by the
regional and period-fixed effects in the placebo regressions do not necessarily threaten
the parallel trends assumption. Employment policies, including informal employment,
are nationally determined by the Department of Labour and Employment (DOLE).
While local governments sometimes perform “crackdowns” on illegal vendors along
major thoroughfares and central areas, these crackdowns simply displace vendors to
other locations rather than shifting them towards formal employment (Kusaka 2017).
2.4.2 Regression Results
Extreme lockdowns have a positive and significant effect on the probability of being
informally employed and this finding is robust to the inclusion of individual-level
controls and regional and period fixed effects. In the full specification, lockdowns raise
the probability of being informally employed by 1.7 pp. Disaggregating by gender,

| 56  |       |             | CHAPTER 2.  | LOCKDOWNS  | AND INFORMALITY |
| --- | ----- | ----------- | ----------- | ---------- | --------------- |
|     |       |             | ((a)) all   | sample     |                 |
|     | ((b)) | males       |             |            | ((c)) females   |
|     |       | Figure 2.4: | Event Study | Regression | Plots           |
To establish that there were no “lockdown” or treatment effects prior to the lockdown
Note:
period,thisfigurepresentseventstudyregressionsaccountingfordynamicleadsandlagsprior
to the treatment period. Moving the treatment period to t-1, parallel trends assumption is
supported with treatment effects being close to zero. This specification includes all relevant
covariates, including period and regional fixed effects. To account for multiple levels of fixed
effects, the reghdfe Stata command developed by Correia (2017) was used.
the DD coefficient is robustly positive and significant only for females, implying that
the effect seen in the full model is largely driven by the increase in the probability of
informal employment among females of about 2.2 pp. Consistent with earlier findings
in the literature of a gendered informalization of work during periods of economic
uncertainty, results in Table 3.3 suggest that among women employed during the
| lockdown, | informality | increased. |     |     |     |
| --------- | ----------- | ---------- | --- | --- | --- |
One potential explanation for the overall informalization of the employed during
the lockdown is the survivalist movies earlier established. Where social assistance

2.4. RESULTS AND DISCUSSION 57
mechanisms and sufficient precautionary household savings are absent, the poor simply
cannot afford unemployment. Thus, they face high pressure to look for employment
opportunities immediately after job loss episodes (Turnham and Eröcal 1990; Udall
and Sinclair 1982). Given that informal employment has lower entry barriers and more
flexible conditions than formal employment, this seems to be a preferred outcome,
instead of unemployment, by people who lose a job.
To establish support for the survivalist motive, various alternative labour market
outcomes of the entire labour force are considered—formally employed, informally
employed, unemployed, and inactive. Using multinomial logistic regression, results
presented in Appendix A.8 suggest that extreme lockdowns significantly increase
the likelihood of being in informal employment relative to unemployment for males,
consistent with the survivalist motive. As further evidence, estimates using a 2020
World Bank survey suggest that there is a slightly higher proportion of households in
lockdownregionsthatreliedonsellingassets/properties,engaginginadditionalincome-
generating work and online selling, and requesting advance payment from employers
as coping mechanisms than in non-lockdown regions (see Appendix Table A.10) (Cho
and Johnson 2022).
Certainly, survivalist motives are not the only mechanism at play. Compositional
changes, as discussed in Section 4.1, also suggest that changes in gender-specific
trajectories of formal and informal employment shares partly explain this difference.
To visually demonstrate this difference, Figure 2.5 shows that compositional changes
in lockdown areas are larger in general, and are steepest among informally employed
females. Whether this is a household coping mechanism or a consequence of insufficient
social support mechanisms should be looked into in subsequent work with more
information on time use and state-to-state transition, as was done by Maurizio et al.
(2023). However, totheextentthattheamplifyingeffectoflockdownsisdemonstrated,
net of the adverse social and economic effects that all areas have experienced due to
COVID, these results are notable.

Figure 2.5: Gender-specific trajectories of formal and informal employment
| 2.4.3 Heterogeneous |     | Effects | and Robustness |     | Analysis |     |
| ------------------- | --- | ------- | -------------- | --- | -------- | --- |
The finding that widespread lockdowns had a positive and significant effect on the
informalization of employment for women, but not for men, begs the question—does
this hold for all women and is this robust to alternative model specifications and
| informal employment | definitions? |           |         |        |     |     |
| ------------------- | ------------ | --------- | ------- | ------ | --- | --- |
| Heterogeneous       | Effects      | Among the | Women’s | Sample |     |     |
The findings from the preceding section show that the positive effect of lockdowns on
the informalization of employment is present only among women. However, this result
might differ based on the marital and parental status of women. Table 2.4 presents the
results of the regression on different sub-samples—single women, married or cohabiting
women with no minor children, and married or cohabiting women with minor children.
Informalization is pronounced among women with co-resident children under the age
of 15 (minor children), who arguably have more childcare responsibilities than women
without or with older or non-dependent children. Oxfam (2021) reports that women
on average perform 6.5 hours of primary care work per day during COVID, more than
double the 2.4 hours done by men on average. In Germany, despite men’s increased
involvement in household work during COVID, women also worked more and still did
the bulk of the tasks (Kreyenfeld and Zinn 2021). Given the unequal distribution
of care work and the flexibility that informal employment offers compared to formal

60 CHAPTER 2. LOCKDOWNS AND INFORMALITY
employment, it is not surprising to find that extreme lockdowns informalize work more
for mothers with minor children than others.
This finding resolves the paid work versus care work dilemma that was potentially
exacerbated by the lockdown. If a mother experiences a job loss during the lockdown,
she can disengage in job search and paid employment to focus on care work. However,
the survivalist motive posits a different outcome for developing countries—despite
the increased demand for care work, individuals cannot afford unemployment, and
inactivity by extension, since they have little to no access to safety nets. This confirms
an earlier finding of Bayudan-Dacuycuy and Dacuycuy (2018) that among females, a
wage increase has a statistically insignificant effect on housework hours—implying that
women try to balance carefully the need to participate in the labor market and care
responsibilities. Results in Table 2.4 show that during extreme lockdowns, mothers of
minor children have a higher probability of being informally employed, which despite
its instability, offers more flexibility.
These results likewise echo the findings in Brazil and Peru that, all else held
constant, women with minor children have a higher probability of being in paid
employment with positive hours (Aragao and Villanueva 2021). There is also little
reason to believe that being informally employed is voluntary or driven by an outright
preference for informality, as prior literature demonstrates that mothers in involuntary
informal employment are simply unable to look for formal opportunities due to family
responsibilities (Rodin et al. 2012). Overall, these results confirm prior expectations
that at the height of these lockdowns, women perform the dual role as workers and
carers of first resort.
Robustness to Alternative Sample Restrictions and Definitions of Informal
Employment
In the main model, the pre-treatment period was limited from the third quarter
of 2016-onwards. Considering that labour market policy is within the ambit of
the national government, only the period of the incumbent administration, which
assumed office in June 2016, was included. Estimating the models on alternative
period restrictions generates the same implications. The lockdown effect on informal
employment is robustly positive and significant when one considers shorter alternative
periodssuchasonlysecondquartersurveysandonlythethreeprecedingquarters. Note
that including periods covering earlier administrations (before 2016 Q3) introduces

Note: The outcome variable is a binary variable indicating informal employment. A minor child is
defined as an individual younger than 15 who is living within the same household as the parent. We
qualify that the child is minor because of the prevalence of co-residence of non-minor adult children
(typically single) with their parents in the Philippines. While children above 15 do usually remain
with the parents, they arguably require less care work and parental supervision than younger children
and, therefore, are less likely to affect the labor market status of the mother. Thus, a married woman
without a child or with a non-minor/adult child who lives in the same household falls under column
(3) while a married woman with a minor child is classified under column (4).
more heterogeneity, because labour market policy and enforcement differ across
administrations. Similarly, considering a shorter period restriction, despite yielding
stronger results, cannot be conceptually justified especially if data availability allows
for longer coverage that would sufficiently establish parallel trends.

62 CHAPTER 2. LOCKDOWNS AND INFORMALITY
In terms of age restrictions, the main model limits the sample to age 15 and above,
consistentwiththedefinitionoftheworking-agepopulationbythePhilippineStatistics
Authority and by most statistical offices (ILO nd). It is also uncommon to place an
upper bound restriction on the working age for developing countries, considering the
prevalence of old age employment. Racelis et al. (2012) shows that over 50 percent of
the elderly report being in paid employment, even after the compulsory retirement age
in the formal sector. An overwhelming majority of this work is informal. Nevertheless,
results hold even under the narrowest sample restriction based on age. By including an
upper bound at 64 years old and by increasing the lower bound to 18 years old, results
are consistent in direction and, to a large extent, magnitude. Appendix Tables A.3and
A.4 show the results of robustness analysis for alternative timeline and age restrictions,
respectively.
Finally, it should be noted that there exist various definitions of informal
employment in the literature. In some definitions, informal employment has an
insurance-based definition (whether one is a contributor to the social security
system) (Madero-Cabib and Cabello-Hutt 2022; Villanueva and Lin 2020). Within
mainland Southeast Asia, there are operational differences in the definition of informal
employment with Thailand incorporating the social security-based definition, whereas
Myanmar and Lao PDR incorporating both social security-based and worker-based
definitions (Ducanes et al. 2023). In the Philippines, a 2008 Informal Sector Survey
allowed for both an enterprise-based definition of employment in the informal sector
and a job-based definition of informal employment (Cabegin 2008). This does not
mean that one approach, more so one article, is superior or necessarily correct. On the
contrary, highlighting and scrutinizing alternative, context-based definitions is critical
inadvancingresearchoninformalemployment. Inthisanalysis, thepreferreddefinition
is both class- and sector-based with an explicit preference for an underestimate, such
that the estimates presented are likely lower bounds and the true lockdown effect might
even be larger.
In Tables B.4 and B.5 of the Supplementary Material, results show that across
all relevant and expanded definitions of informal employment, the lockdown effect
is positive and strongly significant and the gendered informalization finding holds.
Results also hold even when merely using a class-based definition of informal
employment and controlling for or omitting agriculture and government sectors instead
(see Table A.7).

2.5. CONCLUSION 63
2.5 Conclusion
The Philippines imposed what many considered one of the world’s longest and strictest
military-enforced lockdowns in select regions at the onset of COVID. This quasi-
experimental set-up allows the article to uniquely contribute to the literature by
isolating and analyzing the “lockdown effect” on the informalization of employment
and distinguishing between compositional and survivalist explanations underlying this
process. Using a two-way fixed effects difference-in-differences design on pooled LFS
data, results show a positive and significant effect of extreme lockdowns on the
probabilityofbeingininformalemployment. Thisfindingispronouncedamongwomen,
particularly for working mothers with minor children, and is robust to alternative age
and period sample restrictions and definitions of informal employment.
Thearticleexplorescompositionalfactorsandsurvivalistmotivesthathelpexplain
these findings. While compositional changes in the employed population played a
role, survivalist motives also suggest that some workers, males in particular, are more
likely to informally work rather than be unemployed. Around 44% of households in
lockdown regions also engaged in additional income generating work.7 Meanwhile,
gendered differences are also explained by women retaining or taking on paid informal
employment due to increased uncertainty brought about by the lockdowns, on top of
their increased caregiving responsibilities.
One caveat when interpreting these results is that LFS data consistently
undercounts informal employment in developing countries. More specifically, the LFS
mightexcludefromthedefinitionofinformallyemployedthosewageandsalaryworkers
employed in unregistered businesses since the LFS does not contain information about
the employers’ legal status. While results are demonstrated to be robust across
possible alternative definitions, the data is inherently limited in that there is no
information about social security contributions or the legal status of the employer.
Still, this limitation underestimates rather than obscures the causal effect of extreme
lockdowns on the probability of informal employment, so the true effect might actually
be somewhat larger than the estimates presented.
Similarly, further research could look into sector-specific differences in informal
employment given its prevalence in some occupations (e.g. babysitting, house cleaners,
etc.). Absent panel data in the Philippines that allows analysis of individual
trajectories, qualitative work that focuses on individual coping mechanisms regarding
7See own estimates in Appendix Table A.10 using World Bank (2022) survey data.

64 CHAPTER 2. LOCKDOWNS AND INFORMALITY
employment might likewise be insightful.
Limitations aside, these results should be read not only as an analysis of the
gendered effects of COVID, but also as part and parcel of a rich body of literature
demonstrating how informally employed women bear the brunt during periods of crises
and how state responses, or the lack thereof, facilitate informalization (Hammer and
Ness 2021). In light of growing uncertainties, mobility restrictions are becoming
either a consequence (e.g. during natural disasters) or a response mechanism (e.g.
during pandemics). Either way, informal employment rises succeeding these crises
(FeridhanusetyawanandGaduh2000;FloroandDymski2000;MendozaandJara2020;
Pecha Garzón 2017), although some evidence suggests that informal employment does
not always play its usual countercyclical role (Maurizio et al. 2023).
Whereas most of the recent literature has interchangeably treated the "COVID-19
pandemic"and"lockdowns"inbothlanguageandsubstance, thisarticleoffersevidence
that stringently enforced and widespread lockdowns, per se, caused an informalization
of employment for women. This highlights the need to reconsider the design and
targetingofsafetynetsforvulnerablesegmentsinthelabourmarketbut, morebroadly,
challenges any notion or misbelief that employment responses during the pandemic
were only male-driven. On the contrary, the lockdown effect on informalization is
pronounced for women with minor children who also arguably perform more caregiving
responsibilities. Women, therefore, played a dual role of being both a worker and carer
of first resort. Expanding gender-sensitive and targeted active labour market policies
including job search assistance, training, and public employment programs is likely to
proactivelyestablishadditional“layers” ofdefenceagainstcrises-driveninformalization.
While some argue that lockdowns were the “great equalizer” that affected everyone
equally, the article offers evidence that in the case of informal employment, such was
not the case.

Chapter 3
Initial Employment Conditions and
Subsequent Fertility Behavior
ABSTRACT
The conditions upon which people enter the labor market have been demonstrated to
affect a variety of later life outcomes such as family formation, employment, and wealth
accumulation. A growing strand of the literature that emerged after the 2008-09 Global
Financial Crisis has shown that employment uncertainty leads to postponed childbearing
and higher childlessness. Yet it is not only individual job conditions that matter,
broader macroeconomic conditions upon entry also matter. Indeed, the “scarring”
literature has likewise demonstrated how cohort-specific outcomes (e.g. wages, fertility)
of recession entrants are lower when compared to non-recession entrants. Using detailed
employment and birth histories of labor market entrants in Germany, this paper
examines the effects of initial conditions, operationalized using fixed-term employment
and recession year entry, on subsequent fertility behavior. Partly addressing endogenous
selection into initial conditions, we employ a two-step identification strategy combining
a non-parametric optimal full matching step and a parametric event history modeling
step using the matched data. Results suggest that entering the labor market with a fixed-
term contract has persistent negative effects on first births up to a decade after entry
and the results are pronounced only for females, whereas entering during a recession
has persistent negative effects only for males.
Keywords: fertility, labor market entry, fixed-term employment, recession entry
65

66 CHAPTER 3. INITIAL EMPLOYMENT CONDITIONS AND FERTILITY
3.1 Introduction
Doinitialemploymentconditionspersistentlyshapelaterlifeoutcomes? Arichbodyof
the literature overwhelmingly suggests that the conditions upon which people enter the
labor market have been shown to affect subsequent employment (e.g. wages and career
trajectories), family formation (e.g. marriage and fertility), and wealth accumulation
(e.g. home ownership, savings) outcomes (von Wachter 2020b,a; Huckfeldt 2022; Choi
et al. 2020; Blossfeld 1987; Scherer 2004; Witteveen 2017). As such, understanding the
nuances of and factors underlying these patterns has long been of interest in the social
sciences literature, especially during and after periods of economic uncertainty. Among
many relevant outcomes, fertility is of key interest among developed economies with
aging societies such as Germany, given that its steady decline in births has considerable
implications on its pension and healthcare systems as well as workforce composition.
Prior literature demonstrates that entering the labor market with some form
of uncertain employment conditions typically leads to either postponed childbearing,
lowerprobabilitiesofparenthood, orboth(Alderottietal.2021;AuerandDanzer2016;
Hofmann et al. 2017; Schmitt 2021; Vignoli et al. 2020b). Indeed, among young females
in Germany, the probability of childbearing for those with fixed-term contracts is 20
percentage points lower than those with a permanent contract and these differences
persist even after a decade since labor market entry and are also evident in the number
of children (Auer and Danzer 2016). Fixed-term contracts have risen in popularity at
leastinpartduetocallsforflexibilizationinlabormarketsthatontheonehandreduces
lower turnover costs and increases optimal search matches, but on the other hand,
increases inequalities in job security (Gebel 2009). In Germany, fixed-term contracts
are particularly prominent among labor market entrants, who are more willing to take
these positions as “stepping stones" before pursuing more stable permanent careers
later in life. Indeed, Gebel (2009) estimates using panel data from 1989 to 2006 that
about 31% of employed labor market entrants had a fixed-term contract. The main
difference between permanent and fixed-term contracts is the tenure duration, with the
former being open-ended and the latter being time-bound without any guarantee for
renewal or eventual regularization.
In Italy, Vignoli et al. (2020b) estimate, using propensity score matching methods,
the causal effect of first job uncertainty (e.g. fixed-term employment) on first
childbearing within five years and finds that 7 percent of first-birth postponement
of women, and 5 percent of that of men, is attributable to these precarious initial

3.1. INTRODUCTION 67
conditions. In other words, individuals who had precarious first jobs would have had
a first child, or had one earlier, had they secured a more stable job.
Yet it is not only individual working conditions upon labor market entry that
matter for fertility choices, but how they interact with broader macroeconomic
conditions. Indeed, prior work on “scarring effects” has likewise demonstrated how
labor market entry during a recession negatively affects the same life-course outcomes
earlier mentioned, when compared to the outcomes of the non-recession cohorts. In
particular, there has been consistent empirical evidence that those who enter during
a recession experience wage and career trajectory penalties that are persistent and
detectable even after a decade (Cockx 2016; Oreopoulos et al. 2012; Schwandt and von
Wachter 2020). In terms of family formation, labor market entry during a recession
affects not only fertility behavior but also partnership formation and home ownership
(Choi et al. 2020).
The mechanisms through which fixed-term entry and recession entry affects
fertility are somewhat similar. On the one hand, the (objective) “income” effect
captures actual wage differentials—the fact that young workers who just entered
the labor market with a fixed-term contract earn lower on average and thus have
narrower opportunities to achieve financial security and accumulate wealth (Kalleberg
2000; Keller and Seifert 2013; Gebel 2009). Those who entered the labor market
during a recession likewise have lower wages and flatter career trajectories, ceteris
paribus (Oreopoulos et al. 2012; Rothstein 2021). On the other hand, the subjective
“uncertainty” effect pertains to differences in perceptions of economic security and
overall well-being—with those in uncertain employment conditions and those who
enteredduringrecessionsexperiencingmoreworriesandlongerjobsearchspells, among
others (Gebel 2009; Gebel and Giesecke 2009; Van Wijk et al. 2021).
Common in the literature is separating the analysis of these individual and macro-
level manifestations of employment conditions on fertility outcomes. Speaking to these
two broad strands, this paper operationalizes initial conditions in two ways–fixed-
term employment entry and recession entry and differentially examines their effects
on subsequent fertility behavior. We look at the case of Germany, a country that
has experienced an incremental but consistent increase in fixed-term employment since
the reunification, of which 30 to 40 percent are underemployed.1 Using fertility and
1Underemployment is defined as being in involuntary nonstandard employment—those who are
in fixed-term contracts but want a permanent one. The figure came from the public report of the
Statistisches Bundesamt as of June 2022 based on Labor Force Survey estimates.

68 CHAPTER 3. INITIAL EMPLOYMENT CONDITIONS AND FERTILITY
employment histories from the German Socio-Economic Panel (GSOEP), we employ a
two-step identification strategy combining a non-parametric optimal full matching step
and a parametric event history modeling step using the matched data. Doing so has a
couple of advantages to relying either solely on propensity scores-based matching and
weighting techniques or solely on event history models. First, optimal full matching
makes use of the entire sample and creates “subsets" where the distance between the
treated and control subjects is minimized. Second, Austin and Stuart (2015) argue
that its combination with analyzing survival outcomes has rarely been used, even in
epidemiological papers where time-to-event is a common outcome of interest. This
two-step identification strategy of optimal full matching based on propensity scores
and survival analysis has not been done in the fertility literature, to the best of our
knowledge.
In each step of the identification strategy, we analyze different fertility outcomes.
After the first step (matching), we estimate the annual Average Treatment Effects on
the Treated (ATTs) on a tempo measure (e.g. annual childbearing probabilities up to
10 years after labor market entry) and a quasi-quantum measure (e.g. total number of
children after 5 to 20 years). After the second step (Cox Proportional Hazards model),
we estimate hazard ratios in the full and gender-restricted samples to test for aggregate
and gendered differences.
The paper contributes to the literature in two ways. First, it builds on the growing
strand of “causal modeling” in fertility research (Hofmann et al. 2017; Kreyenfeld
2021; Vignoli et al. 2020b) by employing a combination of matching and event history
methods to find a meaningful comparison group among initial labor market entrants.
Intuitively, one compares the fertility outcomes of individuals with a similar propensity
to be “treated” (e.g. work fixed-term or enter during a recession) based on their socio-
demographic characteristics, where one group is treated and the other is not. Thus so,
this paper extends the literature that methodologically employs either pure propensity
scores-based matching and weighting techniques (Hofmann et al. 2017; Vignoli et al.
2020b; Auer and Danzer 2016; Di Nallo and Lipps 2023) or pure event history models
(Schmitt 2012; Kreyenfeld 2015; Schmitt 2021; Van Wijk et al. 2021; Laß 2020). This
is a recent methodological advancement that has been used in epidemiology and public
healthwherematchingisusedasapre-processingstepbeforesurvivalanalysistopartly
address endogenous selection bias (Austin 2011, 2014; Austin et al. 2020a; Austin and
Stuart 2015).

3.2. BRIEF REVIEW OF THE LITERATURE AND HYPOTHESES 69
Our second contribution is conceptually and empirically differentiating between
individual and macro-level conditions as a first step before examining any potential
differences of the individual conditions across the business cycle as has been found
in prior Germany-focused studies on fertility (Di Nallo and Lipps 2023; Hofmann
et al. 2017). In addition, we test whether fixed-term entry and recession entry have a
differential and joint effect on subsequent fertility outcomes not both the entire-sample
and gender-restricted samples.
The rest of the paper is organized as follows: the succeeding section reviews
the takeaways from the employment-fertility and scarring literature and subsequently
develops testable hypotheses. Section 3 presents the data and methods, while Sections
4 and 5 discuss the results and concludes, respectively.
3.2 Brief Review of the Literature and Hypotheses
3.2.1 Fertility Implications of Labor Market Conditions
Labor market conditions, broadly defined, is an umbrella term referring to either
individual working arrangements or the macroeconomic conditions under which one
is working. At the individual level, some working conditions such as part-time
employment, fixed-term contracts, and solo self-employment are more likely to be
insecure, temporary, or precarious. Its relevance has arguably grown in recent
decades, at least in part due to the increased demand for labor market flexibility
(Allmendinger et al. 2013; Kalleberg 2000). Among scholars of fertility behavior, the
operationalization of employment conditions has been varied and context-dependent,
thus so providing us with various perspectives of how different types of uncertainty
(subjective, objective, macro, micro, etc.) affect childbearing. For instance, defining
“employment uncertainty" using both objective and subjective indicators, Schmitt
(2021) and Laß (2020) find that women experiencing some form of employment
uncertainty in Germany have a significantly lower transition to first birth relative to
those with no reported uncertainty, especially for women. However, among women
withlowerlevelsofeducation, uncertaintyacceleratesthistransition(Kreyenfeld2010).
This heterogeneity across educational levels does not seem to be universal—Pailhé and
Solaz (2012) find no such distinctions in the case of France.
This paper operationalizes uncertain initial employment conditions in terms of
fixed-term employment which is relevant in Germany where workers generally strive

70 CHAPTER 3. INITIAL EMPLOYMENT CONDITIONS AND FERTILITY
forstabilityandtenuresecurityandfixed-termworkersaretypicallyworse-off(Giesecke
2009). Fixed-term contracts are more prevalent among labor market entrants in
Germany, covering about 31% on average, from 1989 to 2006 (Gebel 2009). For the
wholepopulation,only7.8%ofworkersareinfixed-termemploymentasof2022,around
90% of whom are in contracts with a duration of less than 3 years.2 Thus, the fixed-
term employment seems to be a bigger concern among labor market entrants, and
whether having this type of contract as a stepping stone has long-term effects.
Along these lines, previous Germany-focused studies have shown that being in
either fixed-term contracts or marginal (part-time) employment reduces the transition
rate to first birth. Except for Gebel and Giesecke (2009) who find no evidence of
a relationship between fixed-term employment and fertility decisions, other papers
find that such conditions have a negative effect on overall childbearing (Auer and
Danzer 2016; Schmitt 2012) and a postponement effect on parenthood transitions
(Schmitt2021). Thesedifferentresults couldbeexplainedbydifferencesinthecontext,
period, analytical samples, and identification strategies employed by these papers,
some of which may not be able to fully address endogenous selection into employment
conditions.
Oflate,theliteraturelargelypointstothenegativeeffectoffixed-termemployment
on fertility in many contexts (Alderotti et al. 2021) including for women in Australia
(Laß 2020) and Southern Europe (Adsera 2011), men in Japan (Piotrowski et al. 2018),
and both sexes in the Netherlands, Germany, and Italy (Schmitt 2021; Van Wijk et al.
2021;Vignolietal.2020b). Themajorityofthesestudiesrelyonpresent-timeindicators
offixed-termemployment,emphasizingtheideathatcurrenteconomicandemployment
conditions matter for fertility behavior. Building on this literature, this paper focuses
on initial employment conditions, particularly entering the labor market with a fixed-
term contract, for two substantive reasons. First, current employment conditions are
path-dependentoutcomeswhichmakesearlierconditionssimilarlyrelevantinanalyzing
fertility. Second, the temporal framing of uncertainty has received renewed attention in
the literature with some papers arguing the understated relevance of future narratives
(Lappegård et al. 2022; Vignoli et al. 2020a). This motivates increased scrutiny not
only of uncertainties of the future but also of those of the past and present to fully
understand both the nuances and points of convergence between and among these
temporal frames. Consequently, the first hypothesis addresses these recurrent findings

In terms of the gendered differences, the literature on employment conditions
and fertility in Germany leans towards demonstrating a negative effect of fixed-term
employment among women (Kreyenfeld 2010; Auer and Danzer 2016; Schmitt 2021).
Note that in Germany, while workers on fixed-term contracts are also entitled to paid
parental leaves, taking it does not extend the period or duration of the contract.
While this may affect the fertility decision-making process, it is unlikely to do so
for labor market entrants because of the temporal spacing between labor market entry
(occurring at around early-mid 20s) and first birth (occurring at around late 20s-
early 30s). Regarding gendered differences, there are two competing mechanisms. On
the one hand, under a breadwinner model where men serve as the main economic
providers of the family, uncertainty in male careers should be more detrimental to
fertility than women’s. On the other hand, as women are the ones who take more
extendedparentalleaves, itismoreimportantforthemtoacquireapermanentcontract
prior to childbearing to avoid the disruption of their careers post-childbirth. As such,

| 3.2.2 |            | On the          | Victims |     | of “Scarring" |        |     |     |
While the preceding section unpacks the role of micro-level employment conditions,
the literature likewise tells us that macroeconomic conditions also matter for various
life course outcomes. Recessionary periods that are characterized by high inflation and
high unemployment, among others, have been demonstrated in the literature to affect
employment and fertility outcomes persistently (Currie et al. 2014; Oreopoulos et al.
2012; Choi et al. 2020; Matysiak et al. 2021b). The “scarring" phenomenon pertains
to workers whose subsequent life-course outcomes are persistently and negatively
impacted by their experiences in the labor market. The literature on scarring can
be grouped into two depending on the “victims"–one strand pertains to workers who

72 CHAPTER 3. INITIAL EMPLOYMENT CONDITIONS AND FERTILITY
suffer from an involuntary job loss or reduction in work hours (“displaced”), while the
other strand encompasses first-time labor market entrants (“entrants”).
In the scarring literature, displaced individuals consequently experience a
reduction in total income in the years of and succeeding a crisis. While some of
thosewhoretainedtheirjobsexperiencedincomelosses, Huckfeldt(2022)demonstrates
that this observed aggregate pattern is driven by “occupation switchers”—those who
transitioned to another occupation after being unemployed. Thus, scarring in this
case pertains to the pronounced and persistent income losses experienced by those
displaced. In terms of fertility outcomes, the effect of job displacement during a
recession varies greatly by population subgroup. Male unemployment has a negative
effect on fatherhood transitions in Germany, Denmark, and England, but the effects of
female unemployment are less clear and consistent (Aksoy 2016; Andersen and Özcan
2021; Kreyenfeld and Andersson 2014; Özcan et al. 2010). Recognizing that the effects
of job displacement on fertility have been extensively studied in the case of Germany
(Hofmann et al. 2017; Di Nallo and Lipps 2023), this paper solely focuses on the
entrants.
Labor market entrants are “victims" in the sense that starting to work during
“bad times” is associated with persistent income losses through lower wages and income
trajectories. Mostly studied among advanced economies (for example, South Korea,
Germany, and the US), the consensus is that individuals who entered during and
after recessions have lower income trajectories (Cockx 2016; Rothstein 2021; Umkehrer
2019; von Wachter 2020a). Interestingly, the literature is somewhat less clear in terms
of family formation outcomes. While women who enter the labor market during a
recession year accelerate the transition to parenthood (Choi et al. 2020; Hofmann
and Hohmeyer 2016), the total number of children of “unlucky entrants” is found to be
higher(Choietal.2020), lower(SchwandtandvonWachter2020), andnotsignificantly
different (Hofmann and Hohmeyer 2016) from those who enter the labor market in
better times. For men, evidence from South Korea points to recession entry having
a negative effect on subsequent marriage and childbearing (Choi et al. 2020). These
studies have of course focused on different contexts and unpacked the nuances of these
aggregate findings.
In this paper, we focus on entrants rather than displaced individuals. Considering
the empirically ambiguous relationship between recession entry and fertility outcomes,
the second hypothesis weighs in on this debate and tests whether a scarring (e.g.

Similarly, a tangential interest in this paper is to understand whether the effects
of recession entry on fertility behavior are different by gender, although prior literature
has been ambiguous in this regard, as discussed above. On the one hand, young adult
males who enter the labor market during a recession and consequently begin with
an uncertain or precarious economic position might be perceived as less desirable in
dating and matching markets and, when coupled with a heightened sense of subjective
economic insecurity, further delay partnership formation and fertility (Currie et al.
2014). On the other hand, young adult females who enter during a recession might
postpone births even later than non-recession entrants as they take a longer time to
stabilize their occupational positions. This extended postponement might ultimately
lead to higher childlessness. This is exactly what was found in the US. Currie et al.
(2014) demonstrate that a one percent increase in the unemployment rate in the year
of labor market entry reduces the completed fertility of that cohort by about 14.2 per
1000 conceptions. As such, while we have no strong priors regarding the direction and
significance of the gendered differences in H2, we consistently adopt the phrasing of
H1a, where women are presumed to value employment stability before childbearing,

phenomenonexistsforworkerswhoenteredonafixed-termcontractduringarecession.
In particular, the negative effect on subsequent fertility experienced by fixed-term
entrants might be more pronounced if they entered during a recession year. For
one, their subjective economic worries are more likely two-pronged– they are worried
about both the temporary nature of their employment on one hand and turbulent
macroeconomic conditions which will shape their future employment opportunities.
