ABSTRACT
The impact of uncertainty on fertility has been widely explored in the literature,
with a strong focus on past and present experiences of both objective and subjective
employment uncertainty. There has been limited research on the effects of caregiving
responsibilities toward a sick parent on fertility decisions. This paper explores
how various employment and caregiving scenarios impact ascribed fertility intentions
(AFI). Using a vignette experiment from the nationally representative German Socio-
Economic Panel Innovation Sample (SOEP-IS) (n=1,750), respondents were randomly
exposed to hypothetical scenarios of couples facing different levels of future caregiving
responsibilities and employment uncertainties who subsequently assessed the likelihood
that the couple would have a child within the next three years. Results show that
having no caregiving responsibilities and no employment uncertainty for both partners
in the foreseeable future increases fertility intentions by 2.8 and 1.9 units, respectively,
on a 0-10 scale, relative to when future caregiving responsibilities and employment
113

114 CHAPTER 5. CAREGIVING- AND EMPLOYMENT-FERTILITY
uncertainties are high. Further testing whether these effects vary by respondents’
characteristics, results lend support to a gendered difference in the effect of caregiving
responsibilities, whereas neither objective nor subjective measures of respondents’
employment markers moderate the effect of future employment uncertainty. This paper
highlights individual perceptions of what scenarios and conditions enable the transition
to parenthood and, more broadly, how caregiving responsibilities and employment
instability translate to concerns about family formation in aging societies.
Keywords: caregiving responsibilities, employment uncertainty, ascribed fertility
intentions, factorial survey experiment, Germany
5.1 Introduction
Families in contemporary societies around the world are confronted with multiple
uncertaintiesandchallengesincludingeconomicandemploymentinstability, caregiving
responsibilities, and growing individual concerns about various issues. The
consequences of these uncertainties for social and economic phenomena such as family
formation and dynamics, wages and labor supply, or political behavior are a pervasive
interest among social scientists, with prior work covering uncertainties in multiple
domains and on various levels—micro and macro, subjective and objective, and short-
term as well as long-term (Currie et al. 2014; Gatta et al. 2021; Goldstein et al. 2013;
Schneider 2015; van Wijk et al. 2022).
Regarding family formation and dynamics, there are strong theoretical arguments
suggesting that uncertainties and anticipated responsibilities in the work and family
domains can lead to fertility postponement and reduced total fertility. However,
empirical evidence is not fully conclusive, as results vary not only by how uncertainty is
operationalized (Alderotti et al. 2021; Buh 2023; Gatta et al. 2021) but also by whether
first birth timing, higher-order births, or fertility intentions are examined (Kreyenfeld
et al. 2023a). Results also differ widely across country contexts. Some studies show
that the association between employment uncertainty and fertility is positive in the US
(Schwandt and von Wachter 2020), ambiguous in Germany (Hofmann and Hohmeyer
2016), and negative in South Korea (Choi et al. 2020). Finally, the literature has also
pointed to heterogeneity across population subgroups, showing that the direction and
strength of the uncertainty-fertility nexus vary, for example, according to gender and
educational attainment (Alderotti et al. 2021; Kreyenfeld and Andersson 2014).

5.1. INTRODUCTION 115
In terms of the operationalization of economic uncertainties, researchers have
mostly focused on either past or current experiences including involuntary job loss,
graduation during a recession, or certain forms of employment arrangements (e.g.
fixed-term contracts). Commonly present in surveys and official national statistics,
objective indicators of unemployment and fixed-term employment are typically used in
operationalizing labor market uncertainty or precarity (Schmitt 2021; Kreyenfeld 2010;
Auer and Danzer 2016). Rarely do survey data consistently include items capturing
future-oriented economic uncertainties that could shed light on people’s assessments
and perceptions of future world states and how they relate to fertility behavior and
intentions.
Vignoli et al. (2020a) argue that narratives and perceptions of the future are
important yet relatively understudied determinants of present-time fertility behavior
and intentions. This framework was pivotal in expanding the uncertainty-fertility
nexus to incorporate the role of future-oriented perceptions. In subsequent empirical
work testing this framework, Lappegård et al. (2022) demonstrate that a positively
portrayed future macroeconomic situation has a positive effect on present fertility
intentions. Further, Guetto et al. (2023b) demonstrate that negative news cycles about
the economy are negatively associated with fertility, suggesting that media plays a role
in shaping individual perceptions of the future and, consequently, fertility decisions.
In this paper, we contribute to the growing literature on the enabling conditions
of family and employment for parenthood transitions (Brauner-Otto and Geist 2018;
Gatta et al. 2021; Vignoli et al. 2020a; Zimmerman et al. 2022), with a focus on two
domains: (1) caregiving responsibilities and (2) employment uncertainty, both with
an explicit orientation towards the future. This is one substantive contribution of our
study to focus on the impact of caregiving responsibilities in addition to economic
uncertainty, whereas most prior work has only considered the latter. This is especially
relevant in aging societies, such as Germany, with an increasing share of “sandwiched”
caregivers (Alburez-Gutierrez et al. 2021) who have dual care responsibilities both
towards aging or sick parents and young children.
Recent demographic trends not only show a decline in birth rates in Europe but
alsothatwomenarepostponingfirstbirths,resultinginoverlappingperiodsofchildand
elderly care (Roustaei et al. 2019; Ester Lazzari and Beaujouan 2024). This scenario
can pose challenges for individuals and families in terms of time, material resources,
and mental and emotional capacities to fulfill caregiving responsibilities. The idea of

116 CHAPTER 5. CAREGIVING- AND EMPLOYMENT-FERTILITY
having competing caregiving responsibilities may affect family formation as couples
seek to avoid compounding the cognitive, emotional, and physical strains of care work.
To date, however, there has been little empirical research on the effects of present and
future caregiving responsibilities on fertility and fertility intentions.
Our study is conducted in Germany which is experiencing persistent increases not
only in the share of elderly in the population but also in childbearing ages. As of 2022,
the median age at first childbearing for women is age 30, and for men age 33 and no
trend reversal seems in sight (DESTATIS 2023). Although healthy life expectancy in
Germany has generally risen to a similar extent as in other countries (EUROSTAT
2023), the postponement of childbearing has increased the risk of people having to
care for their dependent children and their elderly relatives in close succession or even
at the same time in their life courses, placing an increased care burden on mothers in
particular.
In terms of identification, we design a factorial survey experiment with random
assignment of vignettes (i.e., various hypothetical situational contexts and constraints)
to enable causal inference. Survey-based experiments are increasingly popular tools in
theSocialSciencestostudyhumanattitudesanddecision-makingprocessesconsidering
their advantages (Mize and Manago 2022). Through the random assignment of
participantstohypotheticalvignettes, researcherscontrolforthepotentialconfounding
of unobservables (Auspurg and Hinz 2014; Sauer 2020). Survey experiments also
provide an opportunity to explore sensitive topics (including own fertility intentions)
and scenarios that may not be ethically or practically feasible to observe in real-
world settings. Finally, experimental designs address the common issues in inferring
causal claims using observational data such as confounding, response bias (e.g., social
desirability), and sample selection. This paper thus contributes to a growing strand of
research employing comparable experimental methods in studying fertility intentions
(Karabchuk et al. 2022; Lappegård et al. 2022; Guetto et al. 2023a).
Our experiment was fielded in the nationally representative German Socio-
Economic Panel Innovation Sample (SOEP-IS), a subsample from the main GSOEP
respondent pool intended for the implementation of one-off surveys and experiments.
Each respondent received five randomly selected vignettes that varied the future
employment situation of a fictitious heterosexual couple as well as the female partner’s
caregiving responsibilities. Our focus on female caregiving is motivated by persistent
gender inequalities, with women still performing as much as double the care work than

5.2. BACKGROUND AND HYPOTHESES 117
men do (Schäper et al. 2023). Respondents were then asked to rate the likelihood
of each couple having a child in the next three years under these constraints. It is
important to note that the outcome does not directly measure the respondents’ own
fertility intentions but rather the ascribed decisions of the couple in the vignette, as
evaluatedbytherespondents. Therefore,werefertothisoutcomeasascribed fertility
intentions (AFI) for clarity and precision, as has been used in other factorial survey
experiments on fertility (see Guetto et al. (2023a) for instance).
5.2 Background and Hypotheses
This paper builds on three overlapping strands of the uncertainty-fertility nexus
literature—(1) the role of employment uncertainties and caregiving responsibilities; (2)
experimental analyses of the enabling conditions of parenthood; and (3) heterogeneities
across population subgroups. In this section, we briefly summarize the most salient
findings and recent developments in each strand and subsequently highlight our main
contributions.
5.2.1 Employment Uncertainty and Caregiving Responsibilities
On the substantive front, the concept of “uncertainty” as a determinant of fertility
intentions and behavior has been extensively studied. While uncertainty can manifest
and be measured in different ways, the most extensively studied type is employment
uncertainty, which has been analyzed through various measures (e.g., objective and
subjective) and on various levels (e.g., macro and micro). After the 2008 global
financial crisis, objective macro-level measures such as shocks in unemployment rates
are used to demonstrate that, at least in the case of the US, states hit hardest
by the recession experienced a more pronounced dip in fertility (Currie et al. 2014;
Schneider 2015). Subsequent papers operationalized uncertainty beyond labor market
indicators, used Google trends (Comolli and Vignoli 2021) and (traditional) media
coverage (Guetto et al. 2023b) instead, and generally found that economic uncertainty
negatively influences fertility. When uncertainty is operationalized through objective
individual-level markers of unemployment or fixed-term work contracts, more evidence
suggests a negative effect of adverse economic conditions on fertility (Alderotti et al.
2021; Gatta et al. 2021; Hofmann et al. 2017; Modena and Sabatini 2012; van Wijk
et al. 2022).

118 CHAPTER 5. CAREGIVING- AND EMPLOYMENT-FERTILITY
An alternative approach is to use survey data with subjective item batteries
measuring feelings of financial uncertainty or worries regarding one’s own or the
country’s future economic situation (Kreyenfeld, 2010). Studies using such subjective
markers of economic uncertainty have provided mixed results, with some finding
a negative (or postponement) effect (Brauner-Otto and Geist 2018; Hofmann and
Hohmeyer 2013), while others show an acceleration in select population subgroups
such as women with lower levels of education (Kreyenfeld 2015). Alderotti et al.
(2021) provide a comprehensive meta-analysis and show that these mixed results are
due to the variety in measurements of uncertainty, outcomes, country contexts, and
specific population subgroups. By and large, uncertainties experienced by males lead
to postponement of first birth and delay in second birth transitions, while the effect
of female uncertainties on first birth is context- and group-specific. Results for higher-
order births are mixed.
These mixed findings may reflect the theoretically competing income and
substitution effects of economic conditions on fertility. The income effect posits that
having limited resources, as is the case under adverse or uncertain economic conditions,
disincentivizes and lowers fertility. However, limited economic (e.g., labor market)
opportunities also lower the opportunity cost of childbearing, potentially inducing
accelerated or increased childbearing (substitution effect).
While the operationalization of uncertainty in the uncertainty-fertility literature
is predominantly economic, there has been growing interest in other domains including
healthworries (Zimmerman etal.2022), environmental concerns(Bodin andBjörklund
2022), andpolicyaccessworries(Zhangetal.2022). Anotherlessexploreddeterminant
offertilityintheliteratureiscaregivingresponsibilities, whichareincreasinglycommon
in aging societies (towards a sick parent, for instance), disproportionately shouldered
by women. In the case of the US from 2003 to 2012, 8.9% of 25-44-year-old women
are “sandwiched” caregivers who simultaneously care for both a parent or in-law and
a child, compared to 6.5% of men in this age group (Suh 2016). In Germany, 6% of
respondents from 2010 to 2020 who have care responsibilities have dual elderly and
childcare duties based on estimates from the German SOEP—5% for men and 7% for
women (Schäper et al. 2023). This is still a relatively small share. However, with the
steady increase in childbearing ages, this is anticipated to grow. In terms of care work,
broadly defined, women carry the brunt by performing as much as double what men
do (Schäper et al. 2023; DESTATIS 2023). Since sandwiched caregivers experience

5.2. BACKGROUND AND HYPOTHESES 119
worse health outcomes, higher care burden, and more career interruptions (Alburez-
Gutierrez et al. 2021), women who are currently taking care of a parent with care needs
or who expect to do so in the near future might avoid or postpone childbearing to avert
compounded caregiving duties.
In this paper, we therefore substantively extend the uncertainty-fertility literature
by analyzing the effects not only of future-oriented employment uncertainties but
also forthcoming caregiving responsibilities. In effect, we also analyze whether the
simultaneousabsenceoftheseworkandfamily-relatedinstabilitiesyielda“compounded
advantage” for childbearing decisions.
5.2.2 Experimental Analysis of the Enabling Conditions of
Parenthood
On the methodological front, prior research on the uncertainty-fertility nexus has
almost exclusively studied past or current experiences using observational data.
One example of past experiences is the effect of initial labor market conditions
or employment characteristics immediately post-graduation on subsequent fertility
outcomes (Auer and Danzer 2016; Choi et al. 2020; Hofmann and Hohmeyer 2013;
Raymo and Shibata 2017). Using observational data, many of these studies employ
quasi-experimental techniques, leveraging on inter- or intra-cohort differences as
treatment-control groups (e.g., fixed-term vs. permanent, recession vs. non-recession
entrants) and comparing average outcomes. On the other hand, current uncertainty is
typically operationalized as present-time worries using questions such as “How worried
are you about your own economic situation?”. This type of question, as it appears in
the German Socio-Economic Panel, focuses on present-time concerns and at best only
partly and imperfectly captures their perception of the future (Vignoli et al. 2020a).
In other cases, present-time uncertainty is operationalized through the respondents’
current employment status, i.e., whether a respondent is unemployed or holding a
fixed-term working contract (Kreyenfeld 2010; Schmitt 2012, 2021).
This strand of research is worth extending methodologically in two ways. First,
given that there are inherent limitations of inferring causality from observational data
due to possible bias arising from selection and response quality (Winship and Morgan
1999), recent studies have turned to employing quasi-experimental methods such as
difference-in-differences (DiD) and matching and weighting (Hofmann et al. 2017;
ComolliandVignoli2021). However,thesemethodsmaystillnotfullyaddressselection

120 CHAPTER 5. CAREGIVING- AND EMPLOYMENT-FERTILITY
biasif, forexample, thereremainsubstantialresidualdifferencesbetweenthetreatment
andcontrolgroupsaftermatchingorinthecaseofDiDdesigns,whentheparalleltrends
are barely or not at all observed. Social scientists recognize this limitation and are thus
increasingly using experiments (e.g., lab, field) to fully isolate and estimate the causal
effect of a certain variable (Mize and Manago 2022).
Experimental approaches, particularly factorial survey experiments, have been
increasingly used to study ascribed fertility intentions. Recent work has looked at
the effects of policy reforms (Guetto et al. 2023a), narratives of future economic
scenarios (Lappegård et al. 2022), and income and employment conditions (Karabchuk
et al. 2022), and all these papers find the importance of people’s (positive) economic
conditions, both measured objectively and subjectively, in forming the enabling
conditions for parenthood.
The second methodological extension follows through the explicit future
orientation of uncertainty. As discussed above, whereas prior measures of economic
uncertainty focused heavily on past and current experiences, recent work argues that
perceptions or narratives of the future likewise matter. In a seminal work, Vignoli et al.
(2020a) develop a “Narrative Framework” and argue that fertility decisions are based
on a narrative of the future—one’s imagined and perceived future conditions. They
argue that narratives of the future are shaped on the one hand by the individuals’
social network and their interactions (e.g., peers and family), and on the other hand
by external influences and framers of narratives (e.g., media).
Recent work has moved in this direction and empirically tested the effects of
perceptions of the future. Lappegård et al. (2022) use an experimental design
to tease out how future economic scenarios affected fertility intentions in Norway,
operationalizing positive economic scenarios as a country with higher rates of
employment in permanent and full-time contracts and more job opportunities in the
next three years. Respondents who were exposed to these positive economic scenarios
expressedhigherfertilityintentionsthanthoseexposedtoanegativescenarioandthose
not exposed at all (Lappegård et al. 2022).
This paper adopts these two methodological extensions and, in light of the
discussion above, substantively addresses: what are the enabling (e.g., positive)
conditions of parenthood as it relates to one’s caregiving and employment domains?
Under which scenarios or situations are ascribed fertility intentions the highest? We
thus test the hypotheses that:

5.2. BACKGROUND AND HYPOTHESES 121
• H1a (caregiving): Ascribed fertility intentions are higher for couples without any
future caregiving responsibilities.
• H1b (employment): Ascribed fertility intentions are higher for couples without
any future employment uncertainties.
Beyond the separate effects of caregiving and employment domains, we likewise
analyze whether the effect of future employment uncertainty varies at different levels of
future caregiving responsibilities and vice-versa. To analyze whether a “compounded
advantage” exists for couples who face no uncertainties and responsibilities in both
domains such that:
• H2 (simultaneous): Ascribed fertility intentions are highest for couples without
any future caregiving responsibilities and future employment uncertainties.
5.2.3 Heterogeneities by Respondent’s Gender
We also explore whether the effects of caregiving responsibilities and employment
uncertainties vary by the respondent’s gender. We test our priors that the positive
effect of having no future caregiving responsibilities on ascribed fertility intentions is
stronger for female respondents given that they traditionally shoulder these obligations
andthusunderstandthecognitive, emotional, andphysicaldemandsofcaregivingwork
more than men do.
• H3 (gendered differences): The effect of future caregiving responsibilities on
fertility intentions is more pronounced for females compared to male respondents.
Gendered differences in the hypothesized positive effect of having no future
employment uncertainty are more difficult to predict and, indeed, the literature
presents mixed results. Using a quasi-experimental design that exploited an exogenous
variation of economic concerns in Germany, Hofmann and Hohmeyer (2013) found
that economic uncertainty more strongly reduced the fertility of females compared to
males. Meanwhile, the presence of male breadwinner norms would suggest that men’s
employment situation is more central to fertility decisions. Indeed, male employment
uncertainty is associated with delayed first and second-childbearing in Germany and
Denmark (Kreyenfeld and Andersson 2014). These competing predictions might point
either way in terms of whether the positive effect of having no employment uncertainty

122 CHAPTER 5. CAREGIVING- AND EMPLOYMENT-FERTILITY
is larger for male or female respondents, although most studies point to gendered
differences in both fertility and employment patterns. In this paper, we will explore
the gendered differences in the effect of employment uncertainty without strong priors
or expectations about its direction.
5.2.4 Heterogeneities by Respondent’s Own Employment
Markers
One advantage of quasi-experimental or experimental studies conducted on
representative samples is the possibility of examining heterogeneities by population
subgroups. For instance, fertility intentions and behavior vary across employment
types and working conditions. The background for this investigation is that there are
generally strong arguments supporting the notion that labor market uncertainties lead
to fertility postponement. While this may be the result of rational calculations, it may
also be the result of social norms that require individuals to first settle into a stable
employment position before getting married and having children. Prior qualitative
work has shown that these norms are particularly strong in the case of West Germany,
but lesser in the case of the East (Bernardi et al. 2007). Further, the notion that
a secure economic base is a prerequisite for having children may be a more ’middle-
class’ one, so that respondents from lower or more precarious labor market positions
may perceive employment responsibilities less often as a barrier to having children than
higher social classes. This necessitates an examination of whether the treatment effects
are heterogeneous across respondents based on usual markers of employment position
including employment status (e.g. employed vs. unemployed), contract duration (e.g.
fixed-term vs. permanent), and net earnings.
Furthermore, the treatment effects may likewise vary by social class. While
present-day demographic research hardly ever addresses the class-fertility nexus (see
however Albertini et al. (2024); Kreyenfeld et al. (2023a)), classical demography has
extensively been occupied by class differences in fertility behavior. With a strong
moral imperative, this strand of research has stipulated that the fertility behavior of
the lower social classes is typically less subject to careful rational consideration than
that of the higher social classes. Turning the argument around, the belief that having
children requires a stable career may be a historically deep-rooted ’bourgeois’ approach
to fertility choice that is not universal across all social classes.
Finally, it might be argued that it is not the objective markers of employment that

5.3. METHODOLOGY 123
matter but rather the subjective experiences or worries of individuals. These measures
better account for how satisfied or worried individuals are about their own employment
conditions, regardless of what type of employment they have. For instance, someone on
a fixed-term contract may not be worried at all about their employment prospects but
someone on a permanent contract might be worried about, say, their job being prone
to automation or outsourcing. Subjective measures of worries capture these nuances
and have been shown to also be a predictor of fertility (Kreyenfeld 2015; Hofmann and
Hohmeyer 2013). Against this backdrop, we formally test the following hypothesis:
• H4 (heterogeneity by the respondent’s employment markers): The effect of future
employment uncertainty on ascribed fertility intentions is moderated by the
respondents’ own objective and subjective employment markers.
5.3 Methodology
The German SOEP is a nationally representative longitudinal survey covering a broad
set of demographic, social, and economic variables that has been conducted annually
since 1984 (Goebel et al. 2019a). We designed and conducted a factorial survey
experiment in the SOEP Innovation Sample (SOEP-IS), a subsample of the SOEP
that was established in 2011 and is dedicated to the implementation of experimental
and innovative instruments. SOEP-IS respondents only receive a subset of the regular
questionnaire to make space for the additional survey content.
Factorial survey experiments are used to study judgments or decision-making in
complex social situations. A popular approach, and the one we use in this study,
is to expose respondents to different “vignettes” of a case of interest and elicit their
assessment of each case with one or several outcome questions. Vignettes vary in one
or (more typically) several domains of interest (e.g., future caregiving responsibilities
and employment uncertainties). These factors are randomized and respondents are
subsequently exposed to vignettes with different combinations or levels (Auspurg and
Hinz 2014). The vignettes used in the present survey experiment were pre-tested in
late 2021 (Alonso-Perez et al. 2021) and data was collected in the 2022 round of the
SOEP-IS. An advantage of conducting a survey experiment through the SOEP-IS is
that we are able to use standard variables from the SOEP about the respondents’
socio-demographic and economic characteristics including gender, age, marital status,
and own employment information.

| 124                | CHAPTER | 5. CAREGIVING- |     | AND EMPLOYMENT-FERTILITY |
| ------------------ | ------- | -------------- | --- | ------------------------ |
| 5.3.1 Experimental |         | design         |     |                          |
In our experiment, vignettes varied with respect to two attributes of interest, future
caregivingresponsibilitiesandfutureemploymentuncertainty, eachcomprising4levels,
leading to 16 distinct vignettes. For future caregiving responsibilities, the hypothesized
positiveeffectofhavingnosickparentonascribedfertilityintentionsmightbedrivenby
two channels– the positive effect of grandparental support with childcare and of having
no caregiving responsibilities. We limit this confounding in our vignettes by explicitly
designing the scenarios to only capture and refer to the female partner’s caregiving
responsibilities, without any mention of grandparental involvement in childcare. The
implied idea of grandparental support as a “benefit” of having no future caregiving
responsibilities did not come up in the pretests of the experiment (Alonso-Perez et al.
2021). For future employment uncertainty, the treatment levels pertain to which
partner, or if both or none, experiences job loss worries. The experiment began with
a brief contextualization of a fictitious heterosexual couple, Tom and Lena, with the
| following fixed | characteristics: |     |     |     |
| --------------- | ---------------- | --- | --- | --- |
• Tom (age 27/32/37) and Lena (age 25/30/35) have been living together for three
| years | and generally | agree that they | may have | children together; |
| ----- | ------------- | --------------- | -------- | ------------------ |
• Both are currently in stable employment, work full-time, and wish to continue
| doing     | so;         |                 |              |           |
| --------- | ----------- | --------------- | ------------ | --------- |
| • Tom has | no siblings | and his parents | live further | away; and |
• Lena’s older sister and her mother both live close by, while her father died several
| years | ago. |     |     |     |
| ----- | ---- | --- | --- | --- |
Except for age, all of these characteristics were fixed and included to provide the
respondents with additional contextual information about the couple and minimize
ambiguities about the employment and family status of both partners. By providing
constant contextual information, we ensure that respondents think about the same
situation when judging the vignettes. Age was varied across vignettes but the age
difference between Tom and Lena was consistently set to 2 years. The decision to
includethesecharacteristicswasbasedonacognitivepre-testwherevignettesofvarious
characteristics were tested for clarity and brevity (Alonso-Perez et al. 2021).
Each respondent was presented with 5 randomly selected vignettes with varying
future care and employment situations of the couple. After each vignette, the

5.3. METHODOLOGY 125
respondent is asked to evaluate the likelihood of this couple having a child in the
next 3 years on a scale from 0 (very unlikely) to 10 (very likely). To eliminate order
effects (Sauer 2020), vignettes are ordered randomly–respondents are presented with
5 random vignettes from a pool of 16 possible combinations. Below are the exact
wordings of the caregiving and employment situations in the experiment.
The outcome is the respondent’s evaluation of the likelihood that the fictitious
couple will have a child within the next three years, rated on a 0-10 scale. As noted
earlier, this outcome is a measure of ascribed fertility intentions: rather than the
respondents’ own intentions, our experiment captures what their intentions could be
under exactly the same circumstances as the fictitious couple described in the vignette.
While this outcome may not be indicative of the actual behavior, especially among
biological male or female respondents outside childbearing age, our main interest is
not fertility per se but rather the enabling conditions and underlying mechanisms that
influence the fertility decision-making process. As such, our outcomes are presented as
the effect of a certain treatment category (i.e. high employment uncertainty) relative
to a reference category (i.e. no employment uncertainty).
To minimize the burden for the respondents and thus avoid bias due to fatigue,
each respondent evaluated 5 vignettes that were randomly selected from the vignette
universe (16 vignettes). The survey experiment was conducted with a final sample
of 1,750 respondents, who were part of the SOEP-IS. In total, we have collected
8,750 vignette observations with 240 non-responses (2.75%). Each of the randomly
assigned 16 vignette combinations has at least 500 responses, allowing us to make
robust between-group comparisons (Auspurg and Hinz 2014)
While our survey experiment provides a powerful way to randomly “expose”

| 126 | CHAPTER |     | 5.  | CAREGIVING- |     | AND | EMPLOYMENT-FERTILITY |     |
| --- | ------- | --- | --- | ----------- | --- | --- | -------------------- | --- |
respondents to various situational contexts and constraints, it also comes with the
potential limitation that survey experimental responses might not be a good proxy
for respondents’ actual behavior. Hainmueller et al. (2015) reassuringly find that their
estimates from a survey experiment on support for immigrant naturalization are in line
with respondents’ actual political preferences and behavior in real-world elections, but
it remains an open question if and to what extent this congruence of experimental and

The main advantage of conducting this survey experiment on a large and
population-representative sample such as the GSOEP is that it allows us to examine

5.4. RESULTS AND DISCUSSION 127
the differences of the treatment effects by population subgroups (i.e., Conditional
Average Treatment Effects)– an extension that is more difficult to do in smaller and/or
non-random samples (Sauer 2020). To test hypotheses 3 and 4 and explore whether
the treatment effects vary by the respondents’ gender (H3) and the respondents’
own employment characteristics (H4), we estimate interaction models. For H3, we
separatelyrunmodelswithcaregiving-genderandemployment-genderinteractions. For
H4, we interact the employment uncertainty treatment with measures capturing four
objective markers of respondents’ current employment situation: employment status
(i.e., employed full-time, employed part-time, not employed, and others including those
retiredandinactive), contracttype(i.e., fixed-termandpermanent), occupationalclass
(i.e., self-employed, blue-collar, white-collar, civil service, and apprentice/trainee), and
net monthly earnings which divide those with reported net earnings into 5 quintiles. In
thelastthreemarkers,weomitfromthesamplethosereportedasnotemployedtofocus
the comparison between different employment states in each marker among employed
respondents. We likewise present interactions with two subjective markers (financial
satisfaction and own job worries) that capture the role of perceptions and worries,
which are more aligned with the subjective phrasing of the employment uncertainty
treatment in the vignette.
5.4 Results and Discussion
5.4.1 Baseline Results
Results in Figure 5.1 show that having no caregiving responsibilities increases ascribed
fertility intentions on average from 3.0 (SD=2.6) to 5.8 (SD=2.8), or by 2.8 units
(on a 0-10 scale). Having fewer caregiving responsibilities, either through sharing it
with a family member (“some”) or outsourcing them to a facility (“low”) also has a
positive effect on AFIs relative to when the female partner fully shoulders care work.
Indeed, consistent with our expectations of avoiding competing care demands from
aging parents and young children, it is when the female partner has no forthcoming
caregiving responsibilities that AFIs are highest.
On the other hand, couples where both partners do not worry about losing a job
in the future have higher AFIs by 1.9 units relative to couples where both are highly
worried. This finding lends support to the observational literature on the employment
uncertainty-fertility nexus that demonstrates a negative relationship. Results also

With AFIs measured as the likelihood of the couple having a child (in the next three

a couple with no employment uncertainty for both partners and no future caregiving
responsibilities had the highest average AFIs (7.39), while the vignette reporting future

employment uncertainty for both partners and high future care responsibilities had
the lowest average (2.57). It is worth noting that persons who are exposed to high
caregiving responsibilities, but low employment uncertainties still report rather low
AFIs (3.81). The complete summary means for each combination of caregiving and
employment treatments give a sense of the range of AFI values and are presented in
Appendix Figure D.1.

In Figure 5.2, we take a closer look at the interactive effects from the full model
andshowthatthehighestfertilityintentionsareforcoupleswithoutfutureemployment
uncertainty and caregiving responsibilities. An omnibus F test confirms that the
interaction terms are jointly significant (F(9,1712)=8.38, p<0.001). Conversely,
fertility intentions are lowest for couples with both partners experiencing high

130 CHAPTER 5. CAREGIVING- AND EMPLOYMENT-FERTILITY
employment uncertainty and where future female care responsibilities are high. The
difference between these two categories is stark—around 5 units on an 11-point scale.
All these taken together lend support to our second hypothesis that the absence of
multiple and simultaneous uncertainties is where fertility intentions are highest.
5.4.3 Moderating Role of Respondents’ Gender
In this section, we explore whether the effect of future caregiving and employment
uncertainty on fertility intentions is moderated by the respondents’ gender. Results
in Figure 5.3 show some gendered differences in caregiving responsibilities but not
in future employment uncertainty. Consistent with our expectation in Hypothesis
3, formal hypothesis tests reveal that the marginal effect of having low or no
future caregiving responsibilities is significantly higher for female respondents by 0.43
(p=0.002) and 0.27 (p=0.049) units, respectively, compared to males. Indeed, as
females have traditionally disproportionately shouldered caregiving responsibilities,
they might better understand the cognitive, emotional, and physical demands of
care work that are compounded when they are in a situation of having to care for
both an aging parent and a young child. This gendered effect demonstrates how the
caregivingresponsibilitiesofyoungadultstowardsolderparentsaresomewhatabarrier
for childbearing but more for women than men.
On the contrary, we do not find any gendered difference in the positive effect of no
futureemploymentuncertaintyonintentions,oranyoftheotheremploymenttreatment
levels. Thus, contrary to our earlier expectations of seeing gendered differences in both
caregiving and employment, we find this to be the case only for the former where the
effects of certain levels are unambiguously stronger for female respondents.
5.4.4 Moderating Role of Respondents’ Employment Markers
Finally, we address how the effect of future employment uncertainty on fertility
intentions varies based on the respondents’ own objective and subjective employment
markers. As discussed in the preceding section and for completeness, we use all
available and relevant objective employment markers including employment status,
occupational class, contract type, and net monthly earnings, and subjective markers
including financial satisfaction and own job worries. Formally testing this, we
estimate interaction models of employment uncertainty with each employment marker.

5.4. RESULTS AND DISCUSSION 131
Figure 5.3: Interactive effects of caregiving and employment with gender
Note: These models show the predictive margins of the interaction between gender and future
caregiving responsibilities and employment uncertainty, respectively. These estimates control for
Lena’s age and the respondents’ age, gender, marital status, and financial worries. Standard errors
are clustered at the individual level.
Results in Figure 5.4 show that the effects of future employment uncertainty are not
significantly moderated by any of these respondent markers.
Respondents have notably rated the highest fertility intentions for vignettes where
none of the partners have an uncertain job, regardless of their employment status,
contract term, occupational class, and monthly earnings. While recent findings on the
uncertainty-fertility nexus suggest fertility differentials by employment markers such
as occupational class (Kreyenfeld et al. 2023a), the broader absence of a significant
interaction or heterogeneities by employment characteristics may be due to the fact
that respondents were evaluating fertility intentions of the couples in the vignettes
with different preferences and constraints rather than their own situation.
This finding hints that respondents who are asked to make judgments about
other people’s intentions and behavior may be taking into account the context and

132 CHAPTER 5. CAREGIVING- AND EMPLOYMENT-FERTILITY
Figure 5.4: Moderating Effect of Respondent’s Own Objective and Subjective
Employment Markers

each of the six markers of the respondent’s objective and subjective employment characteristics–
employmentstatus(n=7,846)classifiedbetweenfull-time,part-time,andunemployment,employment
contract(n=4,260),occupationalclass(n=4,890),netmonthlyearnings(n=4,630),perceivedfinancial
situation (n=8,485), and subjective job worries (n=5,159). Apart from the employment status and
perceived financial situation interactions, respondents not employed are dropped in the analyses to

environment of the people they are assessing more than their own situation. Thus,
this result suggests that one’s ideas of how other people will behave under a certain
set of constraints are less affected by one’s own situation. We further note that this

5.5. CONCLUSION 133
analysis is descriptive and exploratory–as the covariates of own employment markers
are non-randomly assigned, these are mere associations between each marker and the
treatment effect and not the causal effect of moving from one group (e.g., fixed-term)
to another (e.g., permanent).
5.5 Conclusion
In this paper, we analyze the effects of future caregiving responsibilities and
employment uncertainties on ascribed fertility intentions in Germany. Extending the
survey experiments by Lappegård et al. (2022) and Karabchuk et al. (2022) on the
impact of macro-economic conditions, we conducted a factorial survey experiment
that presented respondents with varying caregiving and employment scenarios faced
by hypothetical couples.
Our contribution is threefold. First, avoiding key limitations of observational
designs (e.g., endogenous selection, desirability, limited measures of uncertainty),
our survey experiment allows us to estimate the causal effect of both caregiving
responsibilities and employment uncertainties on ascribed fertility intentions by
leveraging on the randomized exposure of respondents to vignettes. Second, we extend
the literature on the economic uncertainty-fertility nexus by simultaneously analyzing
how employment worries interact with another and relatively understudied scenario
that is common and relevant for young adults in aging societies such as Germany:
caregiving responsibilities toward a sick parent. Finally, building on Vignoli et al.
(2020a), our vignettes are explicitly framed as responsibilities in and uncertainties
about the future, thus complementing prior work, which has predominantly focused on
past and present experiences of uncertainty (e.g., experiencing a job loss, graduating
during a recession, etc.).
Three findings are worth highlighting. First, couples without female caregiving
responsibilities and employment uncertainties for both partners in the foreseeable
futurehavehigherascribedfertilityintentionsby2.8and1.9units, respectively(ona0-
10scale), relativetowhenbotharehigh. Consequently, wefindthatascribedintentions
are highest when there are no future caregiving responsibilities and employment
uncertainty simultaneously. Second, the positive effect of having low or no future
female caregiving responsibilities is stronger for female respondents, while the effect
of future employment uncertainty does not differ by gender. The gendered gradient

134 CHAPTER 5. CAREGIVING- AND EMPLOYMENT-FERTILITY
in the effect of female caregiving responsibilities might be explained by the fact that
femalescontinuetocarryadisproportionateshareofparentalcaregivingresponsibilities
(Labbas and Stanfors 2023). These expectations may have socialized females to better
understandthecognitive,emotional,andphysicaldemandsofcaregivingwork,andthat
these are particularly pronounced when elderly/parental care occurs simultaneously
with child-rearing. Finally, the effect of employment uncertainty did not seem to be
moderated by the respondents’ employment status, contract type, occupational class,
monthly earnings, financial satisfaction, and own job worries, suggesting that their
expressed fertility intentions are shaped more by their evaluation of the couples’ given
constraints and preferences rather than their own.
The main limitation of our analysis is the inability of the survey experiment to
capture individual fertility intentions and subsequent behavior, an inherent limitation
of vignette designs and survey experiments more broadly. While at least one previous
study (Hainmueller et al. 2015) found a high consistency between survey experimental
and real-world decision patterns, we would caution not to interpret our results in terms
of respondents’ own fertility intentions. Beyond general concerns about a possible
mismatch between survey experimental and real-world behavior, this is due to our
outcome of interest being the intentions ascribed to fictitious couples rather than the
respondents’ own stated intentions. Additionally, our sample is representative of the
German population, which means that a sizable portion of our sample is actually
incapable of biological childbearing either due to their age or sex at birth. Ascribed
fertility intentions as assessed in this study must therefore be interpreted only within
theconstraintssetinthevignetteandnotasindicativeorpredictiveoftherespondents’
own intentions and behavior.
Finally, we recognize the limitation that employment and caregiving constellations
are much more nuanced in reality, and could be far more complex than the four
scenarios included in our experiment. For instance, while our caregiving treatment
levels vary the female’s caregiving workload, these could also be shared between the
partners. It is also possible that these caregiving responsibilities toward a sick parent
occur at a much later age than the early 30s or late 20s in a low-mortality context (e.g.
high life expectancy) such as Germany. Future experimental work can therefore further
explore these nuances in caregiving and employment arrangements, as well as focus on
other outcomes including higher-order births and intentions to remain childless, among
others. Other future avenues of inquiry are possible, including differentiation between

5.5. CONCLUSION 135
havingnocaregivingresponsibilitiesandbenefittingfromcaregivingsupport, analyzing
other forms of uncertainty (e.g., environmental, healthcare, etc.), and comparing the
relative contributions of past experiences and future perceptions on fertility.
Our work speaks to how having competing caregiving responsibilities and high
worries about future employment positions disincentivize childbearing decisions. For
caregiving, our results lend evidence to the idea that the availability of institutional
healthcareandelderlycarefacilitiesthatcouldpartiallyorfullyaugmentthecaregiving
needs of elderly and sick parents may help enable couples to realize their childbearing
goals. On the other hand, policies that minimize people’s worries about the security
of their labor market positions also seem to be beneficial. More broadly, social
policies aimed at supporting families to meet their desired size should take into
consideration tangential policy domains (e.g. in elderly care and labor markets) that
make childbearing conducive and viable for couples.

Armand Garcia1 from the Philippines had been working as a plant operator in
an oil refinery in Saudi Arabia for over a decade until the start of the COVID-19
pandemicwhenhelosthisjob. Unemployedbackinhishometowninoneofthepoorest
neighborhoods in Metro Manila during the height of the lockdowns, he realized that he
was not alone—many of his neighbors also lost their jobs with nothing to do and little
to spend. With whatever little they had—scraps of cloth and old sewing machines,
around 50 of them organized and collectively produced handmade bags, pillows, and
tablecloths and sold them on social media platforms. To this date, their community
organization is slowly but steadily growing online, sometimes participating in local
bazaars and flea markets. Inspired by these familiar stories of resilience during periods
of economic difficulties, the essays in this dissertation are demonstrably driven by the
causes and demographic consequences of employment uncertainty. From these four
| essays,     | six cross-cutting | takeaways | are worth | noting. |     |     |

| 1A randomly | selected          | pseudonym |           |         |     |     |
137

138 CHAPTER 6. CONCLUSION
6.1 Summary and Contributions
1. Employment uncertainty is a loaded term—operationalize with care.
In each chapter, “employment uncertainty” as it appears in the literature seems
to change meaning from one use case to another. This lack of universality in
what scholars mean with the term “uncertainty” or “precarity” is embedded in
the empirical literature. Dating back to seminal theories of social stratification
where Weber et al. (1922) makes a distinction between class, which is determined
by the economic sphere of social interaction, and status, which is shaped by the
identityandhonorassociatedwithpeopleinasociety. Fromthis, andmanyother
contemporary scholars of stratification, we know that employment uncertainty or
precarity could be constructed, understood, and operationalized differently.
This dissertation makes one such attempt. In Chapter 1, a schematic
classification of employment uncertainty is proferred. It is a direct extension
of the earlier work of Blossfeld et al. (2006) who characterize uncertainty in three
dimensions— economic, temporal, and employment-based. In this expanded
schema, wedevelop7maindimensionsofemploymentuncertainty: (1)positional;
(2) durational; (3) statutory; (4) recessionary; (5) class-based; (6) subjective;
and (7) caregiving. Within each dimension, certain groups inherently face more
uncertainty than others.
As indicated in Figure 1.7 and Table 1.7, each chapter deals with multiple
types of employment uncertainty, offering a cross-cutting and transdimensional
assessment of its causes and demographic consequences. In this regard, the
contribution of this dissertation lies in the schematic expansion of employment
uncertainty and the simultaneous operationalization of various dimensions in the
empirical analysis.
2. Employment uncertainty is a determined phenomenon. The analysis
in Chapter 2 builds on the stand of the literature demonstrating the
countercyclicalityofinformality(Maurizio2021), whichtypicallyincreasesduring
periodsofeconomicdownturns(Floroetal.2009;Albertinietal.2019;Fernández
and Meza 2015). Prior work has shown that during and after past episodes of
crises like the 1997 Asian Financial Crisis or the Financial Crises episodes in
Latin America in the early 2000s, unemployment and informal employment rose.
These findings suggest a two-pronged precarization in the labor market as a

6.1. SUMMARY AND CONTRIBUTIONS 139
result of crises: (1) increased joblessness and (2) increased informality among the
remaining employed population.
Therefore,thisdissertationsuggeststhatuncertainemploymentpositions,though
not deemed ideal, serve as risk-coping cushions for individuals who do not
want or cannot afford to be unemployed. While this article demonstrates this
in the Philippine context, the cushioning role of employment uncertainty is
not a developing country-only phenomenon. Among some developed countries
(Germany, in particular), “short time work” or reduced working hours sharply
increased during past episodes of crisis (e.g., Global Financial Crisis and COVID-
19 pandemic) (Naujoks et al. 2022). While uncertain employment positions are
determined, Chapter 2 also shows that this phenomenon is gendered. In the
Philippines, the informalization of employment as a result of extreme lockdowns
was pronounced for women more than men.
Chapter 2 makes two substantive contributions to our understanding of
informality. The first is the conceptual and empirical distinction between the
underlying causes of employment informalization—namely compositional factors
and survivalist motives. Second, it establishes a causal link between state
policies and informalization by isolating the “lockdown” effect from the baseline
recessionary effects of the pandemic. Broadly, this chapter speaks to three
maindimensionsofemploymentuncertaintyasillustratedinChapter1—extreme
lockdowns have had a significant impact on the lowering of employment rates
(positional employment uncertainty) and the increasing of informality within the
working population (statutory employment uncertainty).
3. Employment uncertainty is deterministic. Past, present, and future
experiences matter for fertility decisions.
A sizable portion of this dissertation is devoted to analyzing the demographic
repercussions of employment uncertainty. Chapters 3 to 5 examine how actual
or potential experiences of employment uncertainty at various points in the life
course affect fertility intentions and behavior. This builds on a thick strand of the
literature— from seminal work linking economic conditions and fertility Malthus
(1798); Boserup (1981) to contemporary theoretical models of fertility choice
(Becker 1960) and empirical analyses of employment uncertainty and fertility
outcomes (Kreyenfeld 2010; Alderotti et al. 2021; Matysiak et al. 2021a).

140 CHAPTER 6. CONCLUSION
UsingdataoninitialemploymentconditionsinGermany,Chapter3demonstrates
that females entering the labor market with a fixed-term contract and males
entering during a recession experience persistently lower first birth hazard rates
within a decade after entry compared to their permanent and non-recession
counterpart entrants, respectively. This article highlights how experiences of
past employment uncertainty (e.g., labor market entry that occurs in young
adulthood) affect subsequent life course outcomes.
In terms of measuring present experiences of uncertainty, Chapter 4 shows the
“economic advantage” of males and females in service-class occupations in the
transition to higher-order births, when compared to workers in low and unskilled
social classes. In terms of the role of subjective worries, the article also finds that
among women, there is an independent effect of subjective worries that aren’t
already captured through their occupational class positions.
Finally, Chapter 5 differs from the earlier chapters in three ways— first,
it explicitly frames both (subjective) employment uncertainty and caregiving
responsibilities with a future orientation. Building on the “narratives of
the future” framework (Vignoli et al. 2020a), it highlights the role of
perceptionsandimageriesofthefutureinshapingcurrentpresent-timeintentions
and decisions. Second, it adopts an experimental approach. Employing
a vignette experiment design that randomizes respondents’ exposure to a
hypothetical couple’s future employment and caregiving scenarios allows for
causal identification. Finally, results in this chapter demonstrate that people
experiencing simultaneous uncertainties (having both employment uncertainty
and caregiving responsibilities) have the lowest fertility intentions.
4. Employment uncertainty is determined and deterministic across
different country contexts.
While each chapter of the dissertation identifies local effects based on individual-
level data in specific country contexts, Chapter 1.2 shows that employment
uncertainty is a global phenomenon. Official unemployment rates are persistently
higher in high-income countries, partly owing to robust unemployment assistance
and other welfare support. Among developing countries where unemployment
cannot be afforded and labor markets are less developed, vulnerable employment
isthenorm, ratherthantheexception. DescriptivetrendsinFigure1.1depictthe

6.1. SUMMARY AND CONTRIBUTIONS 141
countercyclicality of unemployment. A sharp increase in the unemployment rate
is notable in high-income economies during the 2008 Global Financial Crisis,
most notably for North America, the epicenter of this recession. Similarly,
unemployment also rose during the political and financial turmoils in the late
90s in Latin America. The observation that certain measures of employment
uncertainty(i.e.,unemploymentrate)riseduringandaftereconomicshocksseems
to travel across contexts. Udall and Sinclair 1982; Lee et al. 2020, see discussions
in.
In terms of the demographic repercussions of employment uncertainty, Chapter
1.3 proffers a global reappraisal. In Table 1.3, the negative effect of a one-unit
increase in the lagged unemployment rate on total fertility rate seems to be
stronger for low and lower-middle-income economies, despite limited country-
specific research in these contexts. Both are robustly negative, but the effect of
unemployment among lower-income countries is almost double the effect size in
higher-income countries. These cross-country estimates lend support to the idea
that, at least at the macro level, positional employment uncertainty and statutory
employment uncertainty contribute to the global phenomenon of fertility decline
(Skirbekk 2008; Karaman Örsal and Goldstein 2018).
In sum, while the examination of developing country contexts in this dissertation
is rather limited, we can deduce two overarching findings that apply to them.
First, developing countries have an inherently different form of employment
uncertainty. Whereas unemployment is the important metric in more advanced
economies, measures of informality and underemployment seem to be more
relevant elsewhere. Second, the negative effect of employment uncertainty on
fertility is stronger in developing countries. Future work could emphasize
whether this difference in magnitude is attributable to cultural or welfare policy-
related differences, or both.
5. Women play a dual role as workers and carers of first resort. One
cross-cutting finding from Chapters 2 and 5 is the dual role that women play as
workers and carers of first resort, rather than women having to give up one role
for the other. In the Philippines, the informalization of women’s employment,
which is most pronounced for mothers with minor children, meant that they
continued to work informally even during the height of extreme lockdowns at the

142 CHAPTER 6. CONCLUSION
onset of the pandemic. During these times, they simultaneously perform paid
and unpaid work rather than trade off one for the other. In Germany, where the
bruntofcaregivingresponsibilitiesarecarriedbywomen, ourvignetteexperiment
shows that lifting caregiving responsibilities towards a sick parent from working
women increases fertility intentions by a significant magnitude (3 units on a 0-10
scale). Building on earlier arguments of Kotowska et al. (2008) and Doepke et al.
(2023), this dissertation lends evidence to the continued blurring of the trade-off
betweenpaidemploymentandcaregivingresponsibilitiesamongwomen. Circling
back to Chapter 1, this dissertation argues that caregiving responsibilities, per
se, constitute an uncertain labor market position as there is inherent uncertainty
as to the length, amount, and intensity of caregiving required. When these
caregiving needs are deemed excessive or unpredictable, these arguably affect the
work domain as well.
6. Novel methodological tools bring fresh perspectives to established
ideas. Each chapter contains a novel methodological contribution to their
respective strands of the literature. In Chapter 2, the use of a two-way
fixed effects difference-in-differences design leverages the regional variation
in lockdown assignment to isolate the lockdown effect, while its militarized
implementation that sealed regional borders automatically addresses spatial
spillovers that are pervasive in area-based treatments. In Chapter 3, the two-
step identification strategy combines a pre-processing non-parametric matching
step with a parametric survival analysis (Cox Proportional Hazards modeling)
step and addresses endogenous selection into initial employment conditions. This
builds on prior fertility literature that has relied either solely on matching-
based or solely on survival analysis-based designs. Chapter 4 employs cure
fraction models to differentiate the fertility effect of social class between
tempo (timing) and quantum (quantity), an approach that has roots also
in epidemiological studies. Finally, Chapter 5 develops a survey experiment
design that randomized respondents’ exposure to a hypothetical couple’s future
employment and caregiving scenarios. This identification strategy addresses
endogeneity issues arising from social desirability and sample selection that are
potentially pervasive in pure survey-based designs exploring this topic.

6.2. A SUBSEQUENT RESEARCH AGENDA 143
6.2 A Subsequent Research Agenda
While the examination of the demographic consequences of employment uncertainty
in this dissertation seems to outweigh the examination of its causes, a future research
agenda that examines why people work in precarious positions in the first place is a
promising path forward. Two extensions come to mind. First, the role of unions and
thecollectivebargaining(CB)processinaugmentinginsufficientstatutorilyguaranteed
minimums (e.g., wages, parental leaves) is well-documented in European and other
welfarestatecontexts. Owingtoalackofcompletepopulation-representativedata, this
is widely understudied in alternative contexts with weaker union presence and social
protectionsystems. Simply,wedon’tknowwhatunionsgetoutoftheCBprocessthat’s
more generalizable than insights from qualitative papers analyzing a very select subset
of CB agreements (CBAs) in specific sectors. One key interest in this project could
be how parental leave provisions are embedded in CBAs. Do CBAs augment parental
leaves already guaranteed by law? In other words, if the law already guarantees X
weeks of parental leave, do CBAs increase it to X + n? To preliminarily answer
this question, we initiated the collection, systematic encoding, and analysis of the
population of all CBAs filed in the Philippines from 2016 to 2021. Our initial estimates
show that around 65% of CBAs contain reinforcing provisions that merely restate the
statutory leave entitlements, while only 5% contain augmenting provisions that provide
higher leaves. In terms of potential mechanisms, semi-structured interviews with union
leaderslendsupporttotheideathatwherecomplianceandenforcementoffamilypolicy
lawsareperceivedasweak, redundancyisasmuchofanobjectiveasaugmentationisin
the collective bargaining process. This work is a natural extension of this dissertation
project. Considering that none of the standalone articles or the developed schema
in Chapter 1 discusses the effects of union membership and collective bargaining in
reducing employment uncertainty, it is interesting to pursue a subsequent research
agenda along these lines.
Relatedly, while working on these dissertation chapters, I more strongly developed
the idea that employment uncertainty, regardless of how it is operationalized (see Table
1.1), is nonrandom. When we talk about fixed-term contracts or being unemployed
or informally employed, these decisions are ultimately made by an agent who has
the discretion and agency to do so. Indeed, employment conditions emanate from
firm behavior. Large and powerful firms are increasingly subjecting their workers
across all levels to restrictive covenants in employment contracts such as non-competes.

144 CHAPTER 6. CONCLUSION
These provisions, which prohibit working for a competitor firm after the term of their
employment, are outright curtailments of workers’ mobility and freedom of occupation
in favor of supposedly safeguarding corporate interests. Lately, this topic has been of
interest among legal scholars and economists, evidenced by a surge of related papers in
their top general and field journals in recent years. Sociologists and demographers have
not explored this topic at all, despite these provisions potentially affecting outcomes of
core interest to ours—employment trajectories, labor market stratification, and family
formation.
Therefore, the claim that there are demographic consequences of employment
uncertainty is, in my view, recurrent but perhaps incomplete. The unstable working
conditions that may hamper family formation do not arise by chance or from workers’
preferences but rather emanate from the actions and policies of firms, willingly
or otherwise. In continental Europe, the outsized hiring and term-setting power
possessed by firms, in the form of more concentrated labor markets, reduces the
probability of hiring on a permanent contract and converting a temporary contract to
a permanent one (Bassanini et al. 2022). However relevant it may seem, the direct link
between firm behavior and demographic outcomes has been relatively understudied.
Building on this dissertation project, a future research agenda that would highlight
the demographic and life course responses to firm behavior using experimental
(and quasi-experimental) methods.
Firms have the bandwidth and discretion to set working hours, establish flexibility
in working time and locations, provide additional leaves where necessary, and even
subsidize childcare. These are de facto and direct firm decisions that may improve the
balance between work and family life. However, other de jure firm policies might have
an indirect effect on demographic decisions. For instance, if the firm restricts one’s
future career opportunities, this might indirectly affect future demographic behavior
as well.
Along these lines, a novel subsequent research agenda could look into the effects
of firms’ imposition of restrictive covenants in employment contracts–provisions
thatexplicitlyrestrictworkersfromworkingorengagingwithcompetitorfirmswithina
certainperiodsucceedingtheiremploymentwiththatfirm. Recentworkshowsthatthe
use of these provisions disproportionately restricts workers’ future career opportunities,
increases job search duration, and generates unnecessary turnover costs because of the
need for retraining (Starr 2019; Starr et al. 2020). Especially worrying are recent

6.3. POLICY IMPLICATIONS AND LIMITATIONS 145
estimates that among private sector employees, 16% (Italy), 19% (Netherlands, UK),
and20%(US,Australia)areboundbysometypeofrestrictivecovenant. Theseoutright
curtailments of and exceptionalities in workers’ mobility and freedom of occupation
potentiallyhaveaccompanyingadverseeffectsonfamilyformationintentions, mediated
by a flatter career trajectory and reduced future employment opportunities.
This could also be tested using a survey experiment that randomizes respondents’
exposure to contracts without and with various types of restrictive covenants through
vignettes and subsequently measures family formation intentions (e.g., fertility,
marriage). Through the conduct of an experiment, one could directly identify
and estimate individual-level responses without encountering the problems of data
incompleteness and recall bias that might be pervasive when analyzing this topic using
survey-based designs. Vignette experiments have also been used to demonstrate the
life course consequences of precarious working conditions (Auspurg and Gundert 2015)
and more recently, the effects of uncertainty on fertility (see Chapter 5) and marriage
(Gong and Wang 2022). Post-exposure, the main outcomes of interest are short- and
long-term marital and fertility intentions and the expressed ideal age for these events.
Additionalquestionsonsubsequentjobsearchintentionsandcareerexpectationswould
allow for a formal mediation analysis that may unpack the mechanisms between firm-
imposed contractual restrictions and demographic behavior.
6.3 Policy Implications and Limitations
Intermsofpolicyimplications, thisdissertation, asacollective, canonlyspeakinbroad
directional strokes. Notably, governments recognize that employment uncertainty
is a pressing concern. From specific legislative packages like the 2022 CHIPS and
Science Act in the United States to country-specific medium-term plans like the
2022-2028 Philippine Development Plan and even internationally agreed targets like
the Sustainable Development Goals, improvements in employment conditions (and a
reduction in uncertainty, more broadly) are deeply incorporated. Therefore, there
doesn’t seem to be a lack of direction and consensus on this issue—policymakers
explicitly strive to foster employment stability. A multi-pronged approach is needed to
achieve such aims.
First, inmanycontexts, strengtheningsocial protection systemsandimproving
targeting towards the most vulnerable groups in the labor market is necessary. In

146 CHAPTER 6. CONCLUSION
the Philippine case, as shown in Chapter 2, among households with a non-working
head, only 0.07% and 0.13% have received job search assistance and livelihood support,
respectively. To reiterate and put it in context, this is less than 1/10th of 1 percent
of all households in the Philippines with a non-working household head. To say that
social protection systems are barely existent, if at all, in some contexts is not an
understatement. Reducingemploymentuncertaintythusnecessitatescontinuouslyfine-
tuning social safety nets.
Second, Chapter 4 highlights the role that upward social mobility (e.g., from
semi/unskilled occupation to a service class occupation, for instance) can play in
increasing higher-order births, especially among men. Governments can play a pivotal
role in this regard by investing in skills development and lifelong learning initiatives
tailored to the demands of the labor market. Some cases of successful upskilling
programs include that of Singapore. Their SkillsFuture initiative includes the provision
of subsidies for training courses and grants for skills acquisition, thereby enabling
upward class mobility and cushioning their local labor market from shocks and other
technological disruptions.
Finally, extending the application of labor market regulations to nonstandard
employment categories also ensures that rules aimed at worker protection do not
discriminate based on type. For instance, in the EU, the recently adopted Platform
Workers Directive aims to clarify the classification of platform workers from self-
employed or independent contractors to direct employees if their actual working
conditions meet certain criteria. This law, therefore, reduces the uncertainty
experienced by platform workers by making them eligible for the same protection and
benefits received by regular and permanent employees. In the Philippines, the 2013
Domestic Workers Act aimed at improving the minimum standards of remuneration
and protection for domestic workers and caregivers, including, most notably, the need
for their employers to contribute to their social insurance premiums. Therefore, this
law removed part of the uncertainty of being informally employed by covering them
under the country’s national social protection system.
While each dissertation chapter has identified its respective limitations, three
overarching ones are worth noting and reflecting upon. First, each chapter presents
estimates that are arguably local and context-specific. As the literature review section
has shown, for instance, there are certain peculiarities in each country’s context that
do not apply elsewhere. For example, Chapters 3 to 5 are all about Germany— a

6.3. POLICY IMPLICATIONS AND LIMITATIONS 147
country that has a marked East/West distinction in social and economic life that has
not yet fully converged even after three decades since reunification (Goldstein and
Kreyenfeld 2011). While these regional disparities have been “controlled for” in each
of these articles, the coefficient sizes and significance for East Germany indicators in
the regression models in Chapters 3 and 4 reveal that it’s still a relevant component in
explaining fertility dynamics in Germany. Therefore, beyond the macro-level analysis
in Chapter 1, one should not infer that the local and context-specific effects detected
in the stand-alone chapters are automatically true in other country contexts.
Further, the dissertation has only tangentially touched on the underlying theories
of change that affect the relationship between the respective “treatments” and
outcomes. In Chapter 2, while the effects are shown to be driven by both compositional
and survivalist factors, this is relatively underdeveloped since the data used (repeated
cross-sections) is not suitable to track individual transitions to different labor market
states. Similarly, in Chapter 3, while alternative specifications robustly demonstrate
the effects of initial conditions on subsequent fertility, there is little discussion as to the
underlying channels (e.g., what happened between entry and fertility?) owing in part
to the relatively small and unbalanced sample in the study. Similarly, in Chapter 5,
whiletheeffectoffuturecaregivingandemploymentuncertaintiesonfertilityintentions
are tested for heterogeneities by gender and employment characteristics, there may be
other personal psychological factors that might also explain the relationship. Moving
forward, subsequent work that extends these stand-alone chapters and looks into
specific mechanisms and more tests for heterogeneity is likely to be insightful.
Finally, the operationalization of some of the variables used in the chapters might
benefit from fine-tuning or comparison with alternative measures. For instance, in
Chapter 3, the recession treatment is a dummy variable that denotes whether the
World Bank and FRED databases classify a certain year as a recession year. However,
Gandrud and Hallerberg (2015) make a point that these dummy indicators do not
capturetheseverityofcrisesnordotheycaptureinstanceswhenearlysignsofeconomic
stress were avoided or managed. Future work that analyzes how workers respond to
brewing economic stress is a promising way forward. Among this novel strand of the
literature is recent work showing that the quantity and emphasis of real-time media
coverage of a country’s (negative) economic situation are negatively associated with
fertility behavior (Guetto et al. 2023b).
In Chapter 5, on the other hand, the caregiving uncertainty treatment is highly

148 CHAPTER 6. CONCLUSION
gendered—fully allocating the care responsibilities unto the female partner whereas
they can be shared in reality between the couple. Thus, further survey experiments
can better map out various caregiving constellations that reflect actual divisions of
paid and unpaid work within families.
In sum, this dissertation demonstrates that employment uncertainty is multi-
dimensional and simultaneously determined and deterministic. People’s labor market
positions cannot be described in a black-and-white manner, nor do they solely predict
life course outcomes by themselves. However, they are a focal component of one’s
identity, perhaps even central for some. This necessitates our continued attention to
and study of these topics in light of changing times and recurrent crises.

A.1 Supplementary Figures

174 APPENDIX A. APPENDIX FOR CHAPTER 2
Figure A.1: Lockdown timeline in the Philippines at the onset of COVID
Source: Pronouncements of the Inter-Agency Task Force for COVID-19 (IATF) and online
reports
Note: This timeline was developed from the pronouncements of the Inter-Agency Task Force
for COVID-19 (IATF), local level executive orders and ordinances, and online news reports.
A blue cell (“none”) implies that within that period (or the most part of it), there were no
region-wide lockdowns. Pink cells (“partial”) denote a region-wide partial lockdown, which
is a less stringent classification than the extreme lockdown. A region is considered partially
locked down if the entire region was placed under a classification below the extreme form
of lockdown (Enhanced Community Quarantine, or ECQ) including Modified Enhanced CQ,
General CQ, and Modified General CQ. A grey cell (“targeted”) means that certain provinces
or cities within that region were under extreme lockdowns while others were not. Finally,
the red cells pertain to areas that underwent the most extreme form of lockdown (ECQ).
As mentioned in the main text, our treatment units are regions that were consistently under
extreme lockdown during the survey period (NCR, Regions III, and IV-A) and our control
units are those that consistently did not impose regional lockdowns during the same period in
favor of targeted ones (Regions 7, 8, 10, 11, and ARMM). The second quarter LFS was held
between the second half of April and the first half of May (yellow box). In the latter part of
the year, the government pursued more localized and targeted solutions rather than blanket
and wide regional shutdowns.

| A.1. SUPPLEMENTARY |     | FIGURES |     | 175 |
| ------------------ | --- | ------- | --- | --- |
Figure A.2: Daily Average Mobility Changes by Lockdown Status, 2020
| Source: Google | (2021) accessed | 05 December | 2021 |     |
| -------------- | --------------- | ----------- | ---- | --- |
Using data from Google LLC (2021) Community Mobility Reports Data, we plot the
Note:
daily average mobility changes in 2020 in lockdown and non-lockdown regions to show that
during the survey period, there is a clear and observable distinction in mobility changes
between treatment and control units. It is evident that lockdown regions had a consistently
larger decline in mobility relative to baseline during the survey period. Note that “non-
lockdown” does not necessarily mean no mobility restrictions at all were present. It simply
means that there was no regional-wide lockdown and any mobility restrictions imposed were
targeted and localized. Consistent with what the timeline in Figure A1.1. shows, these
mobility change differentials dissipate in the latter part of the year as lockdowns became
| more targeted | and nuanced | throughout | the country. |     |
| ------------- | ----------- | ---------- | ------------ | --- |

176 APPENDIX A. APPENDIX FOR CHAPTER 2
Figure A.3: Labor Market Transitions Succeeding Job Loss Episodes
Note: In this framework, we illustrate potential pathways succeeding a job loss. In most of
the labor literature on the implications and effects of the COVID-19 pandemic, the focus has
beenontheextensiveandintensivemargin—increaseinunemploymentandreductioninwork
hours, among others. In this paper, we focus specifically on the succeeding branch—whether
those employed during the pandemic were either formally or informally employed.
