| A.2. ALTERNATIVE |             | INFORMAL | EMPLOYMENT | DEFINITIONS | 181 |
| ---------------- | ----------- | -------- | ---------- | ----------- | --- |
| A.2              | Alternative | Informal | Employment | Definitions |     |
There exist various definitions of informal employment in the literature given that
there are differences in country contexts, data availability, and research questions.
Benanav (2019) offers a historical account of the evolving definitions of “informal
employment” and “employment in the informal sector” led by the ILO and the
contesting interpretations and operationalizations of these measures. Our definition
of informal employment is largely based on the ILO guidance on informal employment
agreed upon at the 17th International Conference of Labour Statisticians (Hussmanns
2004) and encompasses all non-professional, own-account, or contributing family
workers outside agriculture and government. For context, below are examples of some
| definitions | in the recent | literature: |     |     |     |
| ----------- | ------------- | ----------- | --- | --- | --- |
From the table above, it is evident that there is no consensus and universal

182 APPENDIX A. APPENDIX FOR CHAPTER 2
approach in defining informal employment. In many papers cited above, informal
employment has a social security-based definition (whether one is a contributor to
the social security system). Within mainland Southeast Asia, there are operational
differences in the definition of informal employment with Thailand incorporating the
social security-based definition, whereas Myanmar and Lao PDR incorporating both
social security-based and worker-based definitions (Ducanes et al. 2023). This does not
mean that one approach, moreso one paper, is superior or necessarily correct. On the
contrary, highlighting and scrutinizing alternative, context-based definitions is critical
in advancing research on informal employment.
Our definition is different than most ones in the literature that are able to
define informal employment not only from the workers’ job type/class (as we do in
this paper) but also based on the legal structure of their employer (formal/informal
enterprise), their eligibility for paid leaves (eligible/ineligible), and their social security
contributions (covered/uncovered). A more holistic and ideal definition of informal
employment would account for all these factors. The PH Labor Force Survey
unfortunately does not contain information about social security. Notwithstanding,
ourobjectiveinthepaperis nottoprovidethe“closestto thetrue” estimate ofinformal
employment in the Philippines—such an estimate does not exist in the literature nor
in official reports to the best of our knowledge. Rather, we aim to demonstrate the
robustness of our positive, significant, and gendered lockdown effect across
various definitions of informal employment.
With this in mind, Cabegin (2008) argues that “[job-based] figures of informal
employmentareunderestimatedinthattheydonotincludeworkersthatareinformally
employedintheformalsectorandinprivatehouseholds”. Weagreewiththisargument.
However, for the purposes of this paper, the preferred definition we adopt is both class-
(or job-) and sector-based with an explicit preference for an underestimate. An
underestimated informal employment rate means that the estimates we present are
likely lower bounds such that the true lockdown effect might even be larger.
We demonstrate this empirically in the succeeding tables of this section of the
Supplementary Material. In Table A3.1 and A3.2, we show that across all relevant
and alternative definitions of informal employment, the lockdown effect is positive and
stronglysignificantandacrossalldefinitions,thegenderedinformalizationfindingholds
true. For ease of reference, we summarize these estimates below—definitions that yield
a higher IER also yield higher magnitudes of the lockdown effect. Thus, our preferred

A.2. ALTERNATIVE INFORMAL EMPLOYMENT DEFINITIONS 183
definition which is presented in the main text is in light of our aim to demonstrate
credible yet modest estimates of the lockdown effect. Our results also hold even
when merely using a class-based definition of informal employment and controlling for
or omitting agriculture and government workers instead (see Table A3.3).

| ---------- | ----------- | ----------- | --------------- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |

| A.3. | TESTING |     | THE | “SURVIVALIST |     | MOTIVE” | MECHANISM |           |     |     | 187 |
| ---- | ------- | --- | --- | ------------ | --- | ------- | --------- | --------- | --- | --- | --- |
| A.3  | Testing |     | the | “Survivalist |     |         | Motive”   | Mechanism |     |     |     |
The survivalist motive of informal work, particularly relevant among developing
countries, posits that absent social assistance mechanisms and precautionary savings,
workers who are affected by the lockdown are unable to afford unemployment or
inactivity and are thus constrained to seek gainful employment informally. To
test this mechanism of whether lockdowns increase the probability of being in
paid informal employment relative to unemployment, we employ a multinomial
logistic regression model where workers take one of the following employment
categories—formal employment, informal employment, inactivity (outside the labor
force), and unemployment. This approach is useful to estimate the probability of these
outcomes, expressing them relative to other outcomes (Hosmer et al. 2013). Retaining
the same explanatory variables from equation (2) including regional and period fixed
effects, the equation to test this mechanism is parsimoniously specified as:
n
|     | Pr(y = | K −1) |         |      |     |     |          |     |        |       |       |
| --- | ------ | ----- | ------- | ---- | --- | --- | -------- | --- | ------ | ----- | ----- |
|     |        |       |         |      |     |     | (cid:88) |     |        |       | (A.1) |
| ln  |        |       | = α+βLA | +γLP | +δ  | (LA | ∗LP )+   | θ X | +σ +τ  | +ϵ    | ,     |
|     | Pr(y   | = K)  |         | g    | t   | DD  | g t      | j   | jigt g | t igt |       |
j=1
whereKisthebaseoutcomeandK-1asthealternativeoutcomes. Therelativerisk
ratios (RRR) are the exponentiated multinomial logit coefficients from the equation
above and are reported in the table. RRRs compare the risk of individuals in lockdown
relative to those in non-lockdown regions to be in one outcome relative to the base
| outcome, |     | which | is defined | as “unemployment”. |     |     |     |     |     |     |     |
| -------- | --- | ----- | ---------- | ------------------ | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
Results suggest that for individuals in lockdown relative to non-lockdown regions,
the relative risk of being in informal employment relative to unemployment is increased
by 14 percent for males. Simply put, male workers in lockdown regions are more
likely than those in non-lockdown regions to be informally employed relative to
being unemployed. As for females, coefficients for the female dummy in the full
sample are telling—the relative risk of females being in informal employment relative
to unemployment is double relative to males. These findings partly support the
| hypothesized |     | mechanism, |     | and Table | A4  | shows the | full results. |     |     |     |     |
| ------------ | --- | ---------- | --- | --------- | --- | --------- | ------------- | --- | --- | --- | --- |

Note: The table shows relative risk ratios, with unemployment as the base outcome. An RRR greater than 1 for the
coefficient of interest implies that an individual in a lockdown region is more likely to be in that outcome relative to

dummy in the case of full samples, as well as region and period fixed-effects. Sample interpretation in the full sample: in lockdown
regions, the risk of being formally employed relative to being unemployed is decreased by 45 percent relative to non-lockdown
regions ((0.547-1.000)/1.000). Standard errors are reported in parentheses.

Note:
(U˜SD
workers; CAMP is a one-time PHP 5,000 90) financial assistance for
(U˜SD
displaced workers; AKAP is a one-time PHP 10,000 180) financial
assistance for displaced migrant workers; Department of Trade and Industry
(DTI) Livelihood Seeding Program provides in-kind capital/inputs for micro,
small, and medium enterprises. Department of Agriculture (DA) Rice Farmers
Support Program is a PHP 5,000 (U˜SD 90) cash aid for rice farmers. These
estimates show extremely low coverage with some programs covering less
than one-tenth of one percent of households. Notably, programs such as the
Social Amelioration Program and TUPAD have slightly lower coverage in fully
lockdown areas where arguably the effects of COVID are stronger. These
own estimates are consistent with estimates presented in a World Bank report
showing low coverage of social protection programs (Cho and Johnson 2022).
It is also interesting to note that among households with a non-working head,
coverage of ALMPs is likewise low—all programs have a coverage of less than
| 0.2 | percent. |     |     |     |     |     |     |     |     |

B.3. SUPPLEMENTARYANALYSESUSINGANALTERNATIVELMENTRYMEASURE201
| B.3 | Supplementary |         | Analyses | using | an Alternative |
| --- | ------------- | ------- | -------- | ----- | -------------- |
|     | LM Entry      | Measure |          |       |                |
In this section, we present the replications of the figures in the main text (Figures 3.1
to 3.4 using an alternative measure of labor market entry present in the German Socio-
Economic Panel. This alternative measure (PBIO) identifies the year of entry based
on respondents’ answers to the personal biography questionnaire. Appendix Table B.1
shows that while the shares of fixed-term first entrants and recession entrants of both
measures are similar, the PBIO measure has a slightly lower sample size. Results are
consistentforannualATTsoffixed-termfirstjobsandrecessionentryandthegendered
| difference | in the effects | of recession | entry. |     |     |
| ---------- | -------------- | ------------ | ------ | --- | --- |

|     | ((a)) First Birth | Probabilities |     | ((b)) | Number of Children |
| --- | ----------------- | ------------- | --- | ----- | ------------------ |
The matching variables used in these models are socio-demographic pre-treatment
Note:
characteristics including sex, educational attainment at entry, East Germany, age at entry,
| and migration | background. |     |     |     |     |
| ------------- | ----------- | --- | --- | --- | --- |

((a)) All
((b)) Males ((c)) Females
Note: Thesesurvivalcurvesareestimatedwithamaximumexittimecappedat10yearsafter
LM entry. Panels (b) and (c) are group-specific estimates for males and females, respectively.

204 APPENDIX B. APPENDIX FOR CHAPTER 3
Figure B.9: Difference in Survival Probabilities for Fixed-Term Entry using Matched
Sample (PBIO)
((a)) All
((b)) Males ((c)) Females
Note: These plots show the difference in survival probabilities of treated and control groups
over the first 10 years after LM entry. A statistically significant difference between the groups
can be inferred if the shaded blue area (confidence interval width) does not include zero.
Panels (b) and (c) are group-specific estimates for males and females, respectively. These
plots were generated using the R adjustedCurves package (Denz 2023).

B.3. SUPPLEMENTARYANALYSESUSINGANALTERNATIVELMENTRYMEASURE207
Figure B.12: Adjusted Survival Curves from Interaction of Fixed-Term and Recession
Entry (PBIO)
Note: This plot shows the four adjusted survival curves for each combination of fixed-
term/permanent and recession/non-recession entrants. These curves were fitted in post-
estimation of Cox PH interaction models, controlling for the covariates shown in Table 3.
Overlapping confidence intervals suggest no statistically significant difference between group-
specific survival probabilities. These plots were generated using the R adjustedCurves
package (Denz 2023).

During my PhD, I have benefitted from funding support from the Deutsche
Forschungsgemeinschaft (DFG, German Research Foundation)- 390285477/GRK2458.
For other research-related purposes throughout my PhD, I have likewise received
funding support and/or grants from the following institutions: Philippine Competition
Commission (PCC), Deutscher Akademischer Austauschdienst (DAAD), Bocconi
University- Laboratory for Effective Anti-Poverty Policies (LEAP), and the Evidence
| in Governance | and Politics | (EGAP). |

Multiple chapters in this dissertation used the German Socio-Economic Panel, an
excellent longitudinal data source that is representative of the German population.

Data Agreement. More information about the SOEP can be found in this article:
https://doi.org/10.1515/jbnst-2018-0022

| 222 | APPENDIX | E. FUNDING | ACKNOWLEDGEMENTS |
| --- | -------- | ---------- | ---------------- |

Appendix F
List of Papers
This cumulative dissertation includes introductory (Chapter 1) and concluding
(Chapter 6) sections and four stand-alone articles (Chapters 2 to 5), namely:
1. CH 2. Extreme Lockdowns and the Gendered Informalization of Employment:
Evidence from the Philippines
• sole-authored article published at Work, Employment, and Society
available online: https://doi.org/10.1177/09500170241247121
2. CH 3. Initial Employment Conditions and Subsequent Fertility Behavior
• sole-authored article under review (first round)
3. CH 4. Second Birth Fertility in Germany: Social Class, Gender, and the Role
of Economic Uncertainty
• co-authored article with Michaela Kreyenfeld, Dirk Konietzka, and Philippe
LambertpublishedattheEuropean Journal of Population availableonline:
https://doi.org/10.1007/s10680-023-09656-5
4. CH 5. OnAscribedFertilityIntentionsUnderFutureCaregivingResponsibilities
and Employment Uncertainties: Experimental Evidence from Germany
• co-authored article with Michaela Kreyenfeld, Julie O’Sullivan, Enrique
Alonso-Perez, Jan Paul Heisig, and Paul Gellert under review (revise
& resubmit)
223
